# Analysis of the sermon

<!-- section 1 (id=3577, raw_sort=1): عقل اور توحید -->

## Intellect and Divine Oneness

There exists a fundamental distinction between intellect and reasoning, though the two are sometimes conflated. Intellect (al-ʿaql) is the name of an infinite potentiality that transcends the mortal world and penetrates the spiritual realm, encompassing the realities that dwell therein. Reasoning, by contrast, is a finite force that proceeds from one subject to another by means of logic, operating within the circumscribed domain of mental conceptions. Through the luminescence of reason, humankind may perceive absolute realities; yet through reasoning alone, one can only contemplate these absolutes in a limited fashion. The power of reasoning is the servant of intellect—a resource that the intellect deploys according to its needs.

Absolute reality simply *is*. To illustrate this principle, the Qur'ān declares its witness.[^cite-1]

### Intentions and Actions

When the Battle of Jamal (al-Jamal) was about to commence, a Bedouin approached Amīr al-Muʾminīn ʿAlī and inquired, "O Alī! Do you say that God is one?" Those present sought to silence him, deeming the moment inopportune for such a question. Yet Maulānā ʿAlī commanded them to leave the man undisturbed and to permit him to ask, saying: "Surely this man desires knowledge; we are fighting precisely in order that people may come to know!" By this utterance, he made manifest the superiority of intention over outward action, and clarified that knowledge of the Absolute Truth and its monotheism (tawḥīd) is the very purpose of all action, regardless of its external form—whether acts of worship, glorifications (tasbīḥāt), or warfare.[^cite-2] The underlying reality is that monotheistic devotion to the Mighty Lord (⟪ar:توحید رب العزت،⟫) has always constituted the true intention (niyyah) of every outward deed of ʿAlī. His declaration—"so that people may know"—demonstrates that the real motive (⟪ar:علم⟫) for every intention, thought, and movement is the acquisition of knowledge (ʿilm), whether one stands on the battlefield or in the mosque. Therefore, the question of whether Allāh is one remains permissible at all times and in all places.[^cite-3]

In ʿAlī's own era, the three great wars—Jamal (⟪ar:جمل⟫), Ṣiffīn (⟪ar:صفین⟫), and Nahrawān (⟪ar:نحروان⟫)—were fought as responses to precisely this inquiry. The Messenger of Allāh (peace and blessings upon him) declared: "O Alī! You will engage in struggle over the interpretation of the Qur'ān as I have struggled for the revelation of the Qur'ān."

### The Unity of Truth

At the same time, he delivered a sublime sermon on the monotheism of Allāh upon the battlefield itself. To the Bedouin he expounded that it is impermissible to call Allāh "one" in the sense of numerical counting, for He who has no equal cannot be comprehended within the categories of number and enumeration. Moreover, one cannot speak of Him as possessing a partner, since there exists no second to Him (⟪ar:لا ثانی⟫). There can be no other like unto Him.

This is the Manifest Truth (⟪ar:'الحق المبین'⟫). It transcends all things in the universe, yet remains evident to all. Simultaneously, it is both inaccessible and inescapable. By this declaration, ʿAlī made clear to those endowed with spiritual insight that one must not conceive of Allāh as "one" God in comparison to many gods, but rather recognize that He alone is the Real Reality (⟪ar:الحق⟫), whilst all else is mere pretense and illusion.

**Note on Āya:** In Arabic, the word *āya* (sign or verse) signifies a mark, and each mark points toward the one who created it. For instance, the hoofprints of a horse in sand constitute signs of the horse's existence and presence in that place; smoke, similarly, is a sign pointing to fire.

Everything has been created as a sign (⟪ar:'آیت'⟫) and proof (ḥujja) of the existence of Allāh; every phenomenon points to His presence (⟪ar:'ھو'⟫). The very meaning of *taʾwīl* (esoteric exegesis) (⟪ar:تاویل⟫) is "that which returns to the First"; He is the First, the Last, the Outward, the Inward—and all outward multiplicity returns to His Unity (⟪ar:'وحدانیت'⟫) and His Oneness (⟪ar:'أحدیت'⟫), as Allāh declares:

> ⟪quran 41:53⟫
> ⟪ar:‏سَنُرِيهِمْ آيَتِنَا فِى ٱلأٓفَاقِ وَفِىٓ أَنفُسِهِمْ حَتَّىٰ يَتَبَيَّنَ لَهُمْ أَنَّهُ ٱلْحَقُّ ۗ أَوَلَمْ يَكْفِ بِرَبِّكَ أَنَّهُۥ عَلَىٰ كُلِّ شَىْءٍۢ شَهِيدٌ ‎⟫
> *We will show them Our Signs in the universe, and in their own selves, until it becomes manifest to them that this (the Qur'an) is the truth. Is it not sufficient in regard to your Lord that He is a Witness over all things?*

In this verse, Allāh employs the plural form—"signs" and "horizons"—yet for the truth, He uses the singular form and the definite article (al-) (⟪ar:ال⟫), signifying that the multiplicity of all signs in the cosmos and within the soul return to the One Truth, which is singular and indivisible.[^cite-4]

There dwells within the human being a restlessness and yearning that draws us toward the bliss of unity (tawḥīd). The perpetual striving for this oneness constitutes the soul's highest sustenance.

### The Qur'ān and Its Interpretation

For this reason, although the Qur'ān is one, each verse bears multiple layers of interpretation (taʾwīl).

**Interpretation:** Just as ʿAlī is one, the Imāms of his descendants who have become verses (signs) for him are many.

The Imām Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq (peace be upon him) said to one of his companions: "Were I to wish, I could relate to you seventy interpretations of each verse of the Qur'ān."[^cite-5] Allāh taught the Messenger of Allāh (⟪ar:محمد مصطفی صلی اللہ علیہ و آلہ و سلم⟫) the interpretation of the Qur'ān, and the Messenger then taught it to ʿAlī. The Prophet subsequently proclaimed: "ʿAlī is with the Qur'ān and the Qur'ān is with ʿAlī" (⟪ar-quote:علی مع القرآن و القرآن مع العلی⟫).[^cite-6] ʿAlī himself told al-ʿAbbās: "Were I to expound upon the opening chapter (⟪ar:سورۃ الفاتحۃ⟫), seventy camels would be laden with books." Thus ʿAlī stands as the authentic interpreter of the Qur'ān.

**Interpretation:** The Qur'ān is a book inscribed between two covers—Muḥammad and ʿAlī—and all the pages therein refer to the Imāms (peace upon them) who are from their descendants.

### Reason, Revelation, and Spirit

The revelation of Allāh—the Book of the Qur'ān—is mute (⟪ar:'صامت'⟫) and does not speak in its own language. It requires an interpreter to unfold its meaning (⟪ar:لا بُدَّ لَہُ مِن تَرجُماَن⟫). Likewise, to comprehend the divine messages (waḥy), one requires an interpreter or messenger—and this interpreter is intellect itself (⟪ar:عقل⟫). The connection between revelation (⟪ar:وحی⟫) and intellect becomes evident from the saying of the Messenger of Allāh: "The messenger of man is the interpreter of his intellect" (⟪ar-quote:رسول الرجل ترجمان عقلہ⟫). That is, the intellect sends forth a messenger within the human being. The supreme Reality (⟪ar:'حق'⟫) and the divine (⟪ar:'الہ'⟫) cannot be directly understood or described; only through the traces of divine creation can one acknowledge Their existence and manifestation.

Every utterance of Maulānā ʿAlī reflects the Qur'ān. Each word of his moves from the words of the Qur'ān to its revelation, from revelation to its spiritual essence (rūḥ), and from spirit to the One who revealed it. His statements return the words of the Qur'ān through all these degrees to their Origin—the First Intellect (⟪ar:عقل اول⟫). Therefore, He who revealed this Word is simultaneously concealed within ʿAlī's utterances (as to His meaning) and manifested through words.

| The Revelation | Bearer | Interpreter |
| --- | --- | --- |
| al-ʿAql al-Awwal (the First Intellect) | Muḥammad | ʿAlī |
| The Revelation of the Qur'ān | The Words of the Qur'ān | The Meaning of the Qur'ān |
| Allāh | Revelation | Intellect |

Revelation and intellect appear as two distinct entities, and scholars have traditionally categorized them separately:

| Revelation | Intellect |
| --- | --- |
| Convention, Tradition | Nature |
| Imitation | Research, Verification |
| Piety | Inquiry |

Yet in truth they are intimately connected. There exists mutual complementarity; these two distinct entities join to form one unified whole—the very purpose of which is monotheism (tawḥīd) (⟪ar:توحید⟫). The literal meaning of *tawḥīd* is "to make one" or "to unify" (⟪ar:'توحید'⟫). An example: the manifestation of truth or reality through the conjunction of negation and affirmation. If the truth is that Aḥmad possesses one goat, this can only be established by denying that Aḥmad possesses two goats.[^cite-7]

This synthesis of intellect and revelation, to which the "one" reality points, is the spirit (rūḥ) (⟪ar:'روح'⟫) that stands between the two. Intellect alone is limited in its expanse and cannot withstand revelation; yet the spirit, which animates the intellect, possesses unlimited vastness. Only the spirit can receive the messages of revelation, become aware of them, and authenticate their sublimity. It receives these divine messages and conveys them to the intellect, which then processes and refines them. Were this not so, revelation would become merely a compendium of innumerable ordinances and statutes for human will, rather than a creative inspiration awakening the intellect.

Revelation, being multidimensional, cannot be confined to a single dimension of words. The Messenger of Allāh clothed the Revelation of Allāh in verbal form so that spirituality might assume a physical garment, allowing humankind to perceive, hear, and read it through the five senses. Thus the connection between revelation and intellect is established: whereas confinement of meaning within words restricts it, the intellect liberates meaning from verbal constraint. Through the intellect, the outward form of words produces countless streams of inner significance. This principle is illustrated in the narrative of Mūsā: when Mūsā struck the stone with his staff (which represents intellect), twelve springs of meaning gushed forth from it.[^cite-8]

> ⟪quran 2:60⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ وَإِذِ ٱسْتَسْقَىٰ مُوسَىٰ لِقَوْمِهِۦ فَقُلْنَا ٱضْرِب بِّعَصَاكَ ٱلْحَجَرَ ۖ فَٱنفَجَرَتْ مِنْهُ ٱثْنَتَا عَشْرَةَ عَيْنًۭا ۖ قَدْ عَلِمَ كُلُّ أُنَاسٍۢ مَّشْرَبَهُمْ ۖ كُلُوا۟ وَٱشْرَبُوا۟ مِن رِّزْقِ ٱللَّهِ وَلَا تَعْثَوْا۟ فِى ٱلْأَرْضِ مُفْسِدِينَ ‎⟫
> *And (remember) when Musa (Moses) asked for water for his people, We said: "Strike the stone with your stick." Then gushed forth therefrom twelve springs. Each (group of) people knew its own place for water. "Eat and drink of that which Allah has provided and do not act corruptly, making mischief on the earth."*

The greater the refinement and delicacy of the spirit, the more fully it grasps the content of revelation and the more luminous pearls of meaning it extracts from this single shell. The intellect functions as the inner messenger—or inner consciousness—through which one may empirically verify and authenticate the divine messages of revelation. Were this not the case, humankind would be compelled to accept revelation purely through blind adherence (taqlīd).

For this reason, Amīr al-Muʾminīn told Kumayl that one of the reasons Allāh sent forth the Prophets was to awaken the hidden treasures of the intellect. The intellect is quickened and awakened by revelation. Revelation is, in truth, a voice that penetrates the spiritual depths of intellect, awakening and nurturing the seeds of consciousness that Allāh has sown therein from the eternal day of "Am I not your Lord?" Revelation cannot be obtained from anything save the intellect, nor can the treasures buried beneath the soil of intellect be unveiled except through revelation. From this perspective emerges an eternal bond between intellect and revelation, whereby the two may be described as complementary—or at the very least, intellect may be understood as a manifestation of revelation.[^cite-9]

Maulānā ʿAlī's statement concerning the reason for sending the Prophets—namely, the extraction of the hidden treasures of intellect—proves that the spiritual intellect of any human being cannot remain ignorant of the message of revelation; rather, it recognizes it, for the message of Allāh accords with the very essence of intellect. Since both originate from the same Source, the intellect acknowledges the fountain and origin of revelation, recognizing it as emanating from the True Divine (⟪ar:'الہ الحقیقی'⟫).[^cite-10] The divine messages of the Prophets awaken the intellect; these messages cannot therefore be deemed separate from intellect, but rather constitute its true purpose and aim. In this way the spirit (⟪ar:'روح'⟫) may receive these divine messages through intellect and embody them within itself such that the two become one. When this union occurs, the heart becomes capable of "seeing" Allāh. Allāh cannot be perceived with the eyes, but only through the heart—what Amīr al-Muʾminīn calls the certainty of truth (⟪ar:'حق الیقین'⟫).

<!-- section 2 (id=3599, raw_sort=2): عقل اور استدلال کا فرق -->

## The Distinction Between Intellect and Discursive Reasoning

There exists a continuity between the power of intellect and the power of reasoning (istidlāl), yet the two are also distinct. The continuity consists in this: every thought and conception begins with a meaningful image. The power of reasoning leads to ideas that are logical and grounded in reality. It proceeds through contradiction (tafārqa), antinomy (tanāquḍ), negation (nafī), and affirmation (ithbāt) (⟪ar:مفارقۃ⟫), (⟪ar:تناقض⟫), (⟪ar:نفی⟫), (⟪ar:اثبات⟫), thereby cultivating the hidden treasures of intellect by these instruments. Yet there exists a boundary to this reasoning beyond which it cannot proceed. Its limits are confined to material realities and it cannot enter the spiritual realms. To penetrate these spiritual domains, one requires an external guide—revelation (waḥy) (⟪ar:'وحی'⟫)—which may be understood as the inner messenger constituted by intellect (⟪ar:'عقل'⟫).[^cite-11] Through this companion, the intellect steps into the realm of spirituality. Therefore, when Maulānā ʿAlī negates the concepts related to the Divine (⟪ar:'الہ'⟫), he does so because of the inadequacy and weakness of discursive reasoning. This negation becomes the foundation for evoking and absorbing the consciousness hidden within intellect—a consciousness that transcends the limits of mere reasoning. Thus the rejection of finite human imagination provides the basis for confessing infinite divinity. It is negation that leads to ascent, not destruction that leads to descent.

This interplay of negation and affirmation appears in Nahj al-Balāgha (⟪ar:نہج البلاغۃ⟫), wherein he praises Allāh, declaring:

> Praise be to Allāh, whose praise is not attained by those who voice it, whose blessings are not enumerated in their counting, whose diligent servants cannot discharge His due, who is not realized after determination, nor attained by the diving of understanding, whose attribute has no defined limit, no circumscribed adjective, no measured time, no extended duration. He created the creatures by His power, and spread forth the winds by His clemency, and filled the expanse of His earth with stones.

In Amīr al-Muʾminīn's utterances (and in many others), we discern this paradox: Allāh is praiseworthy, yet He cannot be praised; His blessings are present, yet cannot be counted, though all other things admit of enumeration. It is obligatory to fulfill the right of Allāh, yet it cannot be fulfilled. Many of his pronouncements exhibit such apparent contradictions.

Divine attributes cannot be defined (⟪ar:لیس لصفاتہ حد محدود⟫), nor can they be properly described, yet they undeniably exist. The Qur'ān itself describes the attributes of Allāh—He is Generous (⟪ar:'کریم'⟫), Mighty (⟪ar:'عزیز'⟫), Merciful (⟪ar:'رحیم'⟫)—yet Maulānā ʿAlī avers that none of these attributes can be precisely bounded. God is generous, but to what degree? How great is this generosity? Is it the generosity of myself, or of you, or of another? Or is it like theirs?[^cite-12]

He declares: "Purity of faith (al-ikhlāṣ) (⟪ar:أخلاص⟫) consists in the negation of all His attributes from Allāh." On the one hand, we must acknowledge the attributes of Allāh; on the other, we must negate them. The difficulty is that the human mind can conceive of Allāh only through attributed language. Yet ʿAlī negates these very attributes, leaving no avenue for grasping the concept of Allāh. If it were possible to identify His attributes by such words, and if we are obliged to negate these attributes, what is a person to do? Yet from this contradiction of affirmation and negation, a new and profounder level of knowledge emerges. The hidden treasures of intellect are awakened, and humankind is compelled to recognize that knowledge of Allāh surpasses our powers of thought, imagination, reasoning, and deliberation—faculties that only the spirit can perceive, whilst the intellect cannot comprehend. He declares: "My Lord cannot be imprisoned within dimensions, motion, time, and space; He is subtle, yet not subtle in subtlety; magnificent, yet not in majesty; great, yet not in greatness."[^cite-13]

Here again he acknowledges the attributes of Allāh whilst negating them. Allāh possesses these attributes, yet cannot be confined by them; nor can He be reduced to our imaginings and conceptions. These attributes furnish intellect with a certain measure of conception, though this conception remains weak and incomplete. To acknowledge the perfection of these attributes, they must be negated (owing to the deficiency of conceptualization). From the synthesis of the dimensions of negation and affirmation emerges the profundity of knowledge. Yet this profundity cannot be established unless one acknowledges that these dimensions of negation and affirmation are insufficient to accurately mirror this reality. To negate all attributes is to "confess" His infinite Self—the true purpose of the universe and the declaration of sincerity (kalimah al-ikhlāṣ), "There is no god but Allāh" (⟪ar:کلمۃ اخلاص، لا الہ الا اللہ⟫).[^cite-14]

For any concept to be established in the human mind, definition must be delimited. Where definition exists, there must be boundaries and exclusions. Yet the definition of divine attributes is free from these limitations and exclusions, for they are the infinite attributes of the infinite Essence. In a sense, one may place all attributes in the position of negation within the word of sincerity, then recite the Kalīmah: "There is no Merciful but Allāh," "There is no Mighty but Allāh," "There is no Generous but Allāh" (⟪ar:⟪ar:'لا رحمن الا اللہ'⟫La Rahim illa Allah' ⟪ar:'لا عظیم الا اللہ'⟫ ⟪ar:'لا کریم الا اللہ'⟫⟫). In this manner, the apparent negation of attributes becomes an affirmation of their identity. All these attributes are not externally attached to His Essence, but form part of His very Nature, distinguishable only by mental conception (⟪ar:'علم الوجود'⟫).[^cite-15] For this reason Allāh Himself declares:

> ⟪quran 23:91⟫
> ⟪ar:‏مَا ٱتَّخَذَ ٱللَّهُ مِن وَلَدٍۢ وَمَا كَانَ مَعَهُۥ مِنْ إِلَهٍ ۚ إِذًۭا لَّذَهَبَ كُلُّ إِلَهٍۭ بِمَا خَلَقَ وَلَعَلَا بَعْضُهُمْ عَلَىٰ بَعْضٍۢ ۚ سُبْحَنَ ٱللَّهِ عَمَّا يَصِفُونَ ‎⟫
> *No son (or offspring or children) did Allah beget, nor is there any ilah (god) along with Him; (if there had been many gods), behold, each god would have taken away what he had created, and some would have tried to overcome others! Glorified be Allah above all that they attribute to Him!*

Allāh's infinity merits liberation from all imaginary fetters; yet the heart is not incapable of knowing Him. It may perceive Him through its own vision. The heart has eternally been the seat of rational thought and wisdom; the intellect has required its purification. The remembrance of Allāh (⟪ar:'ذکر اللہ'⟫) is, in truth, the wellspring of intellect's purification, which is why ʿAlī declared: "The remembrance of Allāh is the noblest form of worship." Yet here too a paradox emerges: the reality of remembrance cannot be attained unless the worshipper "dissolves" himself in the remembrance of Allāh—what is called annihilation in Allāh (⟪ar:'فنا بااللہ'⟫).[^cite-16]

Intellect is the name of a spiritual faculty, and the spirit (rūḥ) is its origin and the source of life for it. Without intellect, the spirit becomes an unconscious entity; without this true spirit, the intellect tends toward inanimate thoughts. Allāh declares:

> ⟪quran 32:7-9⟫
> ⟪ar:‏ٱلَّذِىٓ أَحْسَنَ كُلَّ شَىْءٍ خَلَقَهُۥ ۖ وَبَدَأَ خَلْقَ ٱلْإِنسَنِ مِن طِينٍۢ ‎ ‏ثُمَّ جَعَلَ نَسْلَهُۥ مِن سُلَلَةٍۢ مِّن مَّآءٍۢ مَّهِينٍۢ ‎ ‏ثُمَّ سَوَّىٰهُ وَنَفَخَ فِيهِ مِن رُّوحِهِۦ ۖ وَجَعَلَ لَكُمُ ٱلسَّمْعَ وَٱلْأَبْصَرَ وَٱلْأَفْـِٔدَةَ ۚ قَلِيلًۭا مَّا تَشْكُرُونَ ‎⟫
> *Who made everything He has created good, and He began the creation of man from clay. Then He made his offspring from semen of worthless water (male and female sexual discharge). Then He fashioned him in due proportion, and breathed into him the soul (created by Allah for that person), and He gave you hearing (ears), sight (eyes) and hearts. Little is the thanks you give!*

The insufflation of the spirit (nafkh al-rūḥ) (⟪ar:'نفخ الروح'⟫) begets life and consciousness. If the spirit is not granted its due, such a life resembles death. Through this spirit, static things are animated, and inanimate objects are awakened and made aware. Through this Divine Spirit, one acquires knowledge not merely of others, but of oneself. It is from the conjunction of this "existence" and "knowledge" that every spiritual concept is established. When this spirit attaches to the body, life emerges; thus knowledge revives it, as Allāh declares:

> ⟪quran 8:24⟫
> ⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيُّهَا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ ٱسْتَجِيبُوا۟ لِلَّهِ وَلِلرَّسُولِ إِذَا دَعَاكُمْ لِمَا يُحْيِيكُمْ ۖ وَٱعْلَمُوٓا۟ أَنَّ ٱللَّهَ يَحُولُ بَيْنَ ٱلْمَرْءِ وَقَلْبِهِۦ وَأَنَّهُۥٓ إِلَيْهِ تُحْشَرُونَ ‎⟫
> *O you who believe! Answer Allah (by obeying Him) and (His) Messenger when he (PBUH) calls you to that which will give you life, and know that Allah comes in between a person and his heart (i.e. He prevents an evil person to decide anything). And verily to Him you shall (all) be gathered.*

ʿAlī has already disclosed that the divine summons (daʿwa) (⟪ar:'وحی'⟫) in which people live is the one that awakens and quickens the hidden treasures of intellect. The intellect, which expresses the spirit, is not merely the name of a mental power, but is founded upon "life" itself. Maulānā ʿAlī teaches that he who is truly wise (ʿāqil) (⟪ar:'عاقل'⟫) is one whose knowledge is not only correct, but whose action is also correct, and whose every effort tends toward the Absolute Truth. Another definition of "wise" is "he who places things in their rightful stations" (⟪ar:'عادل'⟫). This proves that only the wise can be just (ʿādil), for only one who perceives things according to their reality will place them in their proper station. Correct thought, flawless virtue, and authentic being are faculties pertaining to intellect.

<!-- section 3 (id=3578, raw_sort=3): علی کا کمیل ابن زیاد کو خطاب -->

## The Discourse of ʿAlī to Kumayl ibn Ziyād

Kumayl ibn Ziyād an-Nakhaʿī has related that Amīr al-Muʾminīn ʿAlī ibn Abī Ṭālib (peace be upon him) seized his hand and led him to the cemetery. When they had traversed the burial ground and left the city behind, he heaved a sigh and spoke:

⟪ar-quote:قال كُمَيْل بن زياد- أخذ بيدي أميرالمؤمنين علي بن أبي طالب عليه السلام ، فأخرجني إلى الجبّان فلمّا أصحر تنفّس الصّعَدَاء ثمّ قال⟫

O Kumayl, these hearts are vessels. The best of them is that which preserves (its contents). Guard well what I impart to you.

⟪ar-quote:يَا كُمَيْل بْن زِيَادٍ، إِنَّ هذهِ الْقُلُوبَ أَوْعِيَةٌ فَخَيْرُهَا أَوْعَاهَا فَاحْفَظْ عَنِّي مَا أَقُولُ لَكَ⟫

Humankind is composed of three types. First is the scholar versed in divine science (al-ʿālim al-rabbānī). Second is the seeker of knowledge who also treads the path to salvation. And lastly is the common rabble, who follow every caller and bend before every wind. They do not draw light from the radiance of knowledge, nor do they seek refuge in any reliable anchor.

⟪ar-quote:النَّاسُ ثَلاَثَةٌ- فَعَالِمٌ رَبَّانِيٌّ وَمُتَعَلِّمٌ عَلَى سَبِيلِ نَجَاةٍ، وَهَمَجٌ رَعَاعٌ أَتْبَاعُ كُلِّ نَاعِقٍ يَمِيلُونَ مَعَ كُلِّ رِيحٍ، لَمْ يَسْتَضِيئُوا بِنُورِ الْعِلْمِ، وَلَمْ يَلْجَؤُوا إِلَى رُكْنٍ وَثِيقٍ⟫

O Kumayl, knowledge is superior to wealth. Knowledge guards you, whilst you must guard wealth. Wealth diminishes through expenditure, whereas knowledge multiplies through being shared. The fruits of wealth perish as wealth itself decays.

⟪ar-quote:يَا كُمَيْلُ، الْعِلْمُ خَيْرٌ مِنَ الْمَالِ- الْعِلْمُ يَحْرُسُكَ وَأَنْتَ تَحْرُسُ المَالَ، وَالْمَالُ تَنْقُصُهُ النَّفَقَةُ، وَالْعِلْمُ يَزْكُوعَلَى الاْنْفَاقِ، وَصَنِيعُ الْمَالِ يَزُولُ بِزَوَالِهِ⟫

O Kumayl, knowledge is a creed by which one lives. Through it mankind acquires obedience in his lifetime and a noble name after his death. Knowledge is the sovereign, whilst wealth is the subject.

⟪ar-quote:يَا كُمَيْلَ بْنَ زِيَادٍ مَعْرِفَةُ الْعِلْمِ دِينٌ يُدَانُ بِهِ . بِهِ يَكْسِبُ الْإِنْسَانُ الطَّاعَةَ فِى حَيَاتِهِ وَ جَمِيلَ الْأُحْدُوثَةِ بَعْدَ وَفَاتِهِ وَ الْعِلْمُ حَاكِمٌ وَ الْمَالُ مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهِ⟫

O Kumayl, those who hoard wealth perish though they yet live; whilst the learned endure for as long as time endures. Their bodies may vanish, yet their example persists in the hearts. Behold, here is an abundance of knowledge—and Amīr al-Muʾminīn pointed to his breast. I have sought a bearer for it, and I did find one. But either he was one upon whom reliance could not be placed: he would exploit religion for worldly gain, and through Allāh's favor upon him would domineer over His servants, and through Allāh's arguments would rule His devotees. Or he was one obedient to the bearers of truth, yet lacking discernment in his inward nature. At the first whisper of doubt, misgiving would enter his heart.

⟪ar-quote:يَا كُمَيْلُ هَلَكَ خُزَّانُ الْأَمْوَالِ وَ هُمْ أَحْيَاءٌ وَ الْعُلَمَاءُ بَاقُونَ مَا بَقِيَ الدَّهْرُ أَعْيَانُهُمْ مَفْقُودَةٌ وَ أَمْثَالُهُمْ فِى الْقُلُوبِ مَوْجُودَةٌ هَا إِنَّ هَاهُنَا لَعِلْماً جَمّاً وَ أَشَارَ بِيَدِهِ إِلَى صَدْرِهِ لَوْ أَصَبْتُ لَهُ حَمَلَةً بَلَى أَصَبْتُ لَقِناً غَيْرَ مَأْمُونٍ عَلَيْهِ مُسْتَعْمِلًا آلَةَ الدِّينِ لِلدُّنْيَا وَ مُسْتَظْهِراً بِنِعَمِ اللَّهِ عَلَى عِبَادِهِ وَ بِحُجَجِهِ عَلَى أَوْلِيَائِهِ أَوْ مُنْقَاداً لِحَمَلَةِ الْحَقِّ لَا بَصِيرَةَ لَهُ فِى أَحْنَائِهِ يَنْقَدِحُ الشَّكُّ فِى قَلْبِهِ لِأَوَّلِ عَارِضٍ مِنْ شُبْهَةٍ⟫

So neither this nor that sufficed. Either one is enslaved to pleasure, readily led astray by passion; or one is consumed by the passion to amass and hoard. Neither has any regard for religion in any matter. The closest comparison is to grazing cattle. Thus does knowledge die with the death of its bearers.

⟪ar-quote:أَلَا لَا ذَا وَ لَا ذَاكَ أَوْ مَنْهُوماً بِاللَّذَّةِ سَلِسَ الْقِيَادِ لِلشَّهْوَةِ أَوْ مُغْرَماً بِالْجَمْعِ وَ الِادِّخَارِ لَيْسَا مِنْ رُعَاةِ الدِّينِ فِى شَيْ‏ءٍ أَقْرَبُ شَيْ‏ءٍ شَبَهاً بِهِمَا الْأَنْعَامُ السَّائِمَةُ كَذَلِكَ يَمُوتُ الْعِلْمُ بِمَوْتِ حَامِلِيهِ⟫

O Lord! Yet this is not so. The earth is never void of those who uphold Allāh's argument, whether openly and renowned, or in concealment through fear, lest Allāh's arguments and clear signs be rendered void. How many are they, and where are they? By Allāh, they are few in number, yet great in esteem before Allāh. Through them Allāh preserves His arguments and signs, until they entrust them to others like themselves and plant the seeds thereof in hearts resembling their own.[^cite-17]

⟪ar-quote:اللَّهُمَّ بَلَى! لاَ تَخْلُو الاْرْضُ مِنْ قَائِمٍ لله بِحُجَّةٍ، إِمَّا ظَاهِراً مَشْهُوراً، أوْ خَائِفاً مَغْمُوراً لِئَلاَّ تَبْطُلَ حُجَجُ اللهِ وَبَيِّنَاتُهُ. وَكَمْ ذَا وَأَيْنَ أُولئِكَ؟ أُولئِكَ ـ وَاللَّهِ ـ الاْقَلُّونَ عَدَداً، وَالاْعْظَمُونَ قَدْراً، يَحْفَظُ اللهُ بِهِمْ حُجَجَهُ وَبَيِّنَاتِهِ، حَتَّىٰ يُودِعُوهَا نُظَرَاءَهُمْ، وَيَزْرَعُوهَا فِي قُلُوبِ أَشْبَاهِهِمْ⟫

Knowledge has driven them through to the reality of true discernment, and they have touched the spirit of unshakeable certainty (yaqīn). They render easy what the pampered regard as arduous. They become intimate with what the ignorant find strange. They dwell in this world with their bodies present, yet their spirits suspended in the realm on high. They are the viceroys of Allāh upon His earth and summoners (duʿāt) to His religion.

Ah! How I yearn to behold them! Depart now, O Kumayl, wherever you wish.

⟪ar-quote:هَجَمَ بِهِمُ الْعِلْمُ عَلَى حَقِيقَةِ الْبَصِيرَةِ، وَبَاشَرُوا رُوحَ الْيَقِينِ، وَاسْتَلاَنُوامَا اسْتَوْعَرَهُ الْمُتْرَفُونَ وَأَنِسُوا بِمَا اسْتَوْحَشَ مِنْهُ الْجَاهِلُونَ، وَصَحِبُوا الدُّنْيَا بِأَبْدَانٍ أَرْوَاحُهَا مُعَلَّقَةٌ بِالْـمَحَلِّ الاْعْلَى، أُولئِكَ خُلَفَاءُ اللهِ فِي أَرْضِهِ، وَالدُّعَاةُ إِلَى دِينِهِ، آهِ آهِ شَوْقاً إِلَى رُؤْيَتِهِمْ! انْصَرِفْ إذَا شِئْتَ!⟫

<!-- section 4 (id=3579, raw_sort=4): علم اور بصیرت -->

## Knowledge and Insight

In this sermon, he speaks of the blessed servants of Allāh—the Imāms—and declares that they are the guardians of Allāh's religion. By "divine religion," he means the faith that flows from the Almighty, the quintessence of belief, immutable and complete. As he has taught: "Religion is upheld through the knowledge of the truthful scholars." That is, were it not for these scholars (the Imāms) of truth, all traces of religion would be effaced. He then pronounced: "Knowledge has driven them through to the reality of true discernment" (⟪ar-quote:هَجَمَ بِهِمُ الْعِلْمُ عَلَى حَقِيقَةِ الْبَصِيرَةِ⟫).[^cite-18]

As has been noted, though knowledge has forever been concealed within the essence of intellect, it must be quickened and awakened. His employment of the word "assault" or "penetration" (hajama) testifies that this knowledge lies hidden within meanings buried in the depths of words—deeper significances subordinate to surface meanings. This utterance of his points to the graduated degrees of knowledge. Laying his hand upon his breast, he declared: "Here is an abundance of knowledge" (⟪ar-quote:هَاهُنَا لَعِلْماً جَمّاً⟫). The breast patently contains the heart concealed within, yet to reach the heart one must penetrate deeply into the breast. Words may gesture toward hidden truths, but they cannot encompass them.

The Arabic word *hajama* (⟪ar:'ھَجَمَ'⟫) signifies "to assault" or "to breach." The purpose of an army's assault is to penetrate to the heart of the enemy army, situated in its center. Thus the word *hajama* calls us to "investigate"—to attack the surface of words and break through to the depths of the realities concealed within them. When these depths of discernment become embedded, the gates of contemplation open before the intellect, and one knows the veracity of this knowledge, as the Lord proclaims: "He who attains understanding grasps the essence of knowledge" (⟪ar-quote:من فَھِم عَلِمَ گوہر العلم⟫). It takes struggle to reach this depth, as ʿAlī declares: "Knowledge is acquired through learning" (⟪ar-quote:بالتَّعَلُّم یَناَلُ العلم⟫). The intent is that a person should imbed this external knowledge within his own being through intellect, such that the two become one. It is the power of the spirit (rūḥ) (⟪ar:'روح'⟫) that creates this unification (tawḥīd) (⟪ar:'توحید'⟫) of knowledge and essence.

### The Struggle Against the Self

Yet this is only possible when the self is divested of egocentrism (anāniyyah) (⟪ar:أنانیت⟫). The Master of the Universe teaches that the wise one is he who perpetually contends against the base tendencies, lusts, and desires of his own soul (nafs), for all of these transgress against the power of knowledge. The corruptions of the soul resemble clouds that, when they obscure the sky of intellect, weaken vision and render reality obscure. The Qur'ān characterizes these corruptions as "rust" or "corrosion" (rān):

> ⟪quran 83:14⟫
> ⟪ar:‏كَلَّا ۖ بَلْۜ رَانَ عَلَىٰ قُلُوبِهِم مَّا كَانُوا۟ يَكْسِبُونَ ‎⟫
> *Nay! But on their hearts is the Ran (covering of sins and evil deeds) which they used to earn.*

Though misdeeds are morally reprehensible, when viewed from the spiritual perspective, they stand as the antithesis to the realities created by Allāh. Following them obliterates sight and blinds the eye of the heart—that eye through which Allāh is perceived. The truly wise person thus engages in ceaseless struggle against his desires and cultivates virtue. The root of all vice is ignorance; one must wage a mighty struggle with oneself to be rid of it—a struggle which the Prophet (peace and blessings upon him) designated the greater jihād (jihād al-akbar) (⟪ar:'جہاد اکبر'⟫) or the jihād of the self (jihād al-nafs) (⟪ar:جہاد النفس⟫).[^cite-19]

Maulānā ʿAlī has articulated the nature of this contest:

| The wise one contends against his self through knowledge. |
| --- |
| The most powerful is he who masters his own self. |
| Indeed, those who struggle against their souls in the cause of Allāh—their station in the sight of Allāh is that of the martyred. |
| The ultimate battle is the war of man against his own self. |
| He who comes to know his self wages war upon it. |
| No jihād is greater than the jihād of the self. |

He teaches that though this war appears to be against the self, it is in truth for its own good. The intellect (al-ʿaql) (⟪ar:العقل⟫), he says, is the commander of the army of the Merciful (al-raḥmān) (⟪ar:رحمن⟫), while appetite (al-hawā) (⟪ar:الھوی⟫) is the commander of Satan's army. The self stands caught between the two forces and is pulled by both (mutajādhiba baynahumā) (⟪ar:متجاذبہ بینھُما⟫); whichever prevails, the self joins its ranks. This demonstrates the necessity of conquering the self utterly in order to be enlisted in the army of the Merciful (al-raḥmān) (⟪ar:'رحمن'⟫). The goal is not the destruction of the self, but the extirpation of what is appetite (hawā) (⟪ar:'ھوی'⟫) within it. The Qur'ān affirms that the Messenger did not speak from appetite (hawā):

> ⟪quran 53:3-4⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَمَا يَنطِقُ عَنِ ٱلْهَوَىٰٓ ‎ ‏إِنْ هُوَ إِلَّا وَحْىٌۭ يُوحَىٰ ‎⟫
> *Nor does he speak of (his own) desire. It is only an Inspiration that is inspired.*

Intellect is the spokesman of the army of the Most Merciful, the army that battles against Satan's forces. The Qur'ān speaks of appetite (hawā) in yet another manner:

> ⟪quran 25:43⟫
> ⟪ar:‏أَرَءَيْتَ مَنِ ٱتَّخَذَ إِلَهَهُۥ هَوَىٰهُ أَفَأَنتَ تَكُونُ عَلَيْهِ وَكِيلًا ‎⟫
> *Have you (O Muhammad PBUH) seen him who has taken as his ilah (god) his own desire? Would you then be a Wakîl (a disposer of his affairs or a watcher) over him?*

> ⟪quran 45:23⟫
> ⟪ar:‏أَفَرَءَيْتَ مَنِ ٱتَّخَذَ إِلَهَهُۥ هَوَىٰهُ وَأَضَلَّهُ ٱللَّهُ عَلَىٰ عِلْمٍۢ وَخَتَمَ عَلَىٰ سَمْعِهِۦ وَقَلْبِهِۦ وَجَعَلَ عَلَىٰ بَصَرِهِۦ غِشَوَةًۭ فَمَن يَهْدِيهِ مِنۢ بَعْدِ ٱللَّهِ ۚ أَفَلَا تَذَكَّرُونَ ‎⟫
> *Have you seen him who takes his own lust (vain desires) as his ilah (god), and Allah knowing (him as such), left him astray, and sealed his hearing and his heart, and put a cover on his sight. Who then will guide him after Allah? Will you not then remember?*

The concealed idolatry (shirk al-khafī) (⟪ar:'شرک خفی'⟫) of appetite (hawā) is mentioned when the Messenger of Allāh declared: "Idolatry will be hidden among you more subtly than the crawling of an ant" (⟪ar-quote:الشرك فيكم أخفی من دبيب النمل⟫). To shatter the idols of egocentrism (anāniyyah) (⟪ar:أنانیت⟫), one requires an external force independent of the self. This force consists of the divine sciences stemming from intellect and revelation. Allāh proclaims:

> ⟪quran 79:40-41⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَأَمَّا مَنْ خَافَ مَقَامَ رَبِّهِۦ وَنَهَى ٱلنَّفْسَ عَنِ ٱلْهَوَىٰ ‎ ‏فَإِنَّ ٱلْجَنَّةَ هِىَ ٱلْمَأْوَىٰ ‎⟫
> *But as for him who feared standing before his Lord, and restrained himself from impure evil desires, and lusts. Verily, Paradise will be his abode.*

The core energy of the soul cannot be annihilated, but it can be redirected away from the countless satanic desires and toward the Beneficent. Tawḥīd (monotheism) literally signifies "to make one" or "to unify." Allāh is not only singular (aḥad) (⟪ar:'أحد'⟫) in Himself, but also in His creative activity (⟪ar:أحد⟫). In this sense, monotheism is not only the acknowledgment of Allāh's singularity (aḥadiyyah) (⟪ar:أحدیت⟫) but also a declaration of one's own singularity (aḥad) (⟪ar:'أحد حقیقی'⟫), such that one may properly reflect Him. To know Allāh is to be bound to Him. One meaning of intellect is "binding," for it encompasses and contains meaning. In contrast stands the heart (al-qalb) (⟪ar:الحام⟫), which is the seat of intellect and radiates spiritual realities. In other words, intellect captures; the heart radiates. Intellect binds together the various elements of the soul, unifying them. Therefore the Creator of the Universe declares:

"He who knows Allāh becomes unified with Him" (⟪ar-quote:مَنْ عَرَفَ اللہ تَوَحَّدُ⟫)
"He who knows himself becomes detached from himself" (⟪ar-quote:⟪ar-quote:مَنْ عَرَفَ⟫ Abstraction ⟫)
"He who knows people becomes detached from people" (⟪ar-quote:⟪ar-quote:مَنْ عَرَفَ⟫ People are different⟫)
"He who knows the world restrains himself from it" (⟪ar-quote:⟪ar-quote:مَنْ عَرَفَ⟫ Al-Dunya Tazhd ⟫)[^cite-20]

### Purity of Intention

In another passage, Maulānā ʿAlī designates this complete alignment of the self with divine reality as *ikhlāṣ* (sincerity or purity of intention). The Arabic word *khaluṣa* (⟪ar:خَلُصَ⟫) signifies to be entirely purified, to descend into essence, or to be unadulterated. In this sense he declares: "Purify for Me your knowledge and deeds, your love and hatred, your gaining and renouncing, your speech and silence" (⟪ar-quote:أخلص لی اللہ العلم و العمل والحب و العداوۃ، والقبض و المغادرۃ، والنطق و الصمت⟫).[^cite-21] The meaning of sincerity is to cleanse oneself entirely for the sake of Allāh such that no stain remains. By stain is meant everything other than Allāh (⟪ar:'غیر اللہ'⟫). That is, the purification of all devotion, action, intention, and passion from everything save Allāh is the definition of sincerity. The most damaging of such stains is egocentrism (anāniyyah) (⟪ar:أنانیت⟫). Sincerity demands that every action, thought, and mode of being be selfless and for the sake of Allāh alone. ʿAlī employed the term "final battle" to stress precisely this point. Whoever develops such sincerity becomes a martyr (shahīd) (⟪ar:شہید⟫) in the sight of Allāh, for he has slain his self in the way of Allāh. He lives his life by the light of reason and sustains himself through revelation, as Allāh has declared:

> ⟪quran 3:169⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَا تَحْسَبَنَّ ٱلَّذِينَ قُتِلُوا۟ فِى سَبِيلِ ٱللَّهِ أَمْوَتًۢا ۚ بَلْ أَحْيَآءٌ عِندَ رَبِّهِمْ يُرْزَقُونَ ‎⟫
> *Think not of those who are killed in the Way of Allah as dead. Nay, they are alive, with their Lord, and they have provision.*

There is yet another meaning of shahīd: the one who bears witness. The one perfected in sincerity, through the sacrifice of his soul, testifies that he belongs to Allāh and rejects all that is other than Allāh. His focus is no longer upon himself; rather, Allāh becomes the center of all his concerns. Thus sincerity is existence for the sake of Allāh, and the fullness of Allāh, as He declares:

> ⟪quran 6:162⟫
> ⟪ar:‏قُلْ إِنَّ صَلَاتِى وَنُسُكِى وَمَحْيَاىَ وَمَمَاتِى لِلَّهِ رَبِّ ٱلْعَالَمِينَ ‎⟫
> *Say (O Muhammad PBUH): "Verily, my Salat (prayer), my sacrifice, my living, and my dying are for Allah, the Lord of the 'Alamîn (mankind, jinns and all that exists).*

### Sincerity and Prayer

Prayer constitutes an integral aspect of sincerity. The sustenance of the human spirit is worship, and no nourishment surpasses prayer. This is why, in this verse and numerous others, Allāh mentions prayer foremost. One who neglects prayer deprives his spirit of its sustenance and fails to fulfill its due. He who cannot fulfill his own rights will never be capable of fulfilling the rights of others. Whoever sets matters aright between himself and Allāh, Allāh will set matters aright between him and His servants. The Qur'ān affirms this principle:

> ⟪quran 15:99⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱعْبُدْ رَبَّكَ حَتَّىٰ يَأْتِيَكَ ٱلْيَقِينُ ‎⟫
> *And worship your Lord until there comes unto you the certainty (i.e. death).*

If the hidden treasures of intellect are to be awakened, the pursuit of knowledge must be undertaken with consistency and complete dedication. Such dedication demands a focus on divine realities and a profound form of worship—worship in which no barrier stands between the seeker and the Sought. Only in such worship, wherein the servant forgets all else, even himself, does true benefit accrue; mechanical worship bears no fruit. Maulānā ʿAlī teaches that the example of one who prays without diligence is like one who shoots an arrow without a bow. Likewise, knowledge depends upon action; lacking right action, knowledge is lost. The heart cannot assimilate knowledge without earnestness; consequently it lacks insight and cannot perceive Allāh.

The Master of the Universe declares: "Acquire knowledge, and knowledge will claim you as its own." At first, one is unaware of life's realities. One acquires knowledge that awakens consciousness. Yet when this is pursued with sincerity over time—that is, when one continually translates knowledge into action—a moment arrives when one does not merely integrate knowledge into oneself; rather, true life itself absorbs you. The process one initially performed through great exertion and effort becomes a natural rhythm of life, something one unconsciously undertakes with the same dedication. It is as if habit transforms into instinct.[^cite-22]

Let us return to the saying of the Master: knowledge is driven through to the reality of true discernment. The scholar (ʿālim) (⟪ar:'عالم'⟫) is not merely immersed in discernment; rather, knowledge (ʿilm) (⟪ar:'علم'⟫) itself becomes a means through him, for he has integrated himself into this knowledge.[^cite-23]


<!-- section 5 (id=3580, raw_sort=5): یقین کی روح -->

## The Spirit of Certainty

Then you say, "⟪ar-quote:وبشِّرُ روح الیقین⟫ (They rejoice because they are attached to the Spirit of Faith)." When human beings speak of closeness or intimate relationship with something, the Arabic word ⟪ar:'مباشرت'⟫ (mubaasharāt, direct contact) derives precisely from this meaning. Yet the word carries another dimension: an extreme and overwhelming joy. When a person touches the spirit of faith (rūḥ al-yaqīn), the joy he receives transcends all utterance. This happiness surpasses any other blessing of Paradise, for which Allah says:

> ⟪quran 9:72⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَعَدَ ٱللَّهُ ٱلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَٱلْمُؤْمِنَتِ جَنَّتٍۢ تَجْرِى مِن تَحْتِهَا ٱلْأَنْهَرُ خَلِدِينَ فِيهَا وَمَسَكِنَ طَيِّبَةًۭ فِى جَنَّتِ عَدْنٍۢ ۚ وَرِضْوَنٌۭ مِّنَ ٱللَّهِ أَكْبَرُ ۚ ذَلِكَ هُوَ ٱلْفَوْزُ ٱلْعَظِيمُ ‎⟫
> *Allah has promised to the believers—men and women—Gardens under which rivers flow to dwell therein forever, and beautiful mansions in Gardens of 'Adn (Eden Paradise). But the greatest bliss is the Good Pleasure of Allah. That is the supreme success.*

Maulana Ali remarks that for a scholar to acquire knowledge and contemplate upon it is itself a blessing. Allah the Exalted is One, and He has created each being singular and unique. Every human being possesses an identity in which no other shares. He is alone in this circle, undivided by caste or condition. Therefore, the true knowledge of man is knowledge of his own self—a knowledge he cannot fully grasp. The Messenger of Allah (upon him be peace and blessing) thus said: ⟪ar-quote:من عرف نفسہ فقد عرف ربہ⟫ "Whoever knows his own soul, verily he has known his Lord."[^cite-4] In the early stages of acquiring knowledge, a person's happiness resembles that of one returning from the exile of ignorance to the peace of his home. The wisest among humans is he who is most alive, and the most alive is he nearest to Allah—for life itself is a joy! This happiness differs entirely from the pleasures one obtains through temporal means.

Maulana Ali describes the ⟪ar:'فقیہ'⟫ (faqīh, the jurist and scholar of divine science) as one who does not disappoint people with regard to Allah's mercy. He remains lost in the light of Allah's compassion, so he never feels despair. He is confident that whatever Allah deals with him is clothed in divine mercy. Allah says:

> ⟪quran 15:56⟫
> ⟪ar:‏قَالَ وَمَن يَقْنَطُ مِن رَّحْمَةِ رَبِّهِۦٓ إِلَّا ٱلضَّآلُّونَ ‎⟫
> *[Ibrahim (Abraham)] said: "And who despairs of the Mercy of his Lord except those who are astray?"*

> ⟪quran 6:54⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَإِذَا جَآءَكَ ٱلَّذِينَ يُؤْمِنُونَ بِـَٔايَتِنَا فَقُلْ سَلَمٌ عَلَيْكُمْ ۖ كَتَبَ رَبُّكُمْ عَلَىٰ نَفْسِهِ ٱلرَّحْمَةَ ۖ أَنَّهُۥ مَنْ عَمِلَ مِنكُمْ سُوٓءًۢا بِجَهَلَةٍۢ ثُمَّ تَابَ مِنۢ بَعْدِهِۦ وَأَصْلَحَ فَأَنَّهُۥ غَفُورٌۭ رَّحِيمٌۭ ‎⟫
> *When those who believe in Our Ayat (proofs, evidences, verses, lessons, signs, revelations, etc.) come to you, say: "Salamun 'Alaikum" (peace be on you); your Lord has written Mercy for Himself, so that, if any of you does evil in ignorance, and thereafter repents and does righteous good deeds (by obeying Allah), then surely, He is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful.*

The joy that flows from Allah's mercy constitutes, in truth, the definition of beauty (jamāl). This beauty is not aesthetic in the conventional sense; rather, it is authentic beauty—the imagination that arises from the intellect itself. The Arabic word ⟪ar:'أحسان'⟫ (iḥsān, excellence) commonly denotes virtue or moral perfection, yet it also carries the meaning of ⟪ar:حُسن⟫ (ḥusn, beauty)—concepts that render the soul itself beautiful.[^cite-5] Maulana Ali teaches that the excellence of intellect lies in its capacity ⟪ar:ینظر جمال الظاہر والباطن⟫ to perceive the beauty of all things, both outward and inward. The loveliness of external forms and actions is perfected by their inner beauty. In the same way, the beauty of deeds arises from the beauty or excellence of the soul. This is why the Prophet (upon him be peace and blessing) declared: "Allah is beautiful and loves beautiful things."[^cite-6]

By cultivating beautiful conduct in oneself, a person becomes able to love Allah, for Allah loves beautiful things. By means of these noble manners, Muhammad al-Muṣṭafā (upon him be peace and blessings upon him and his family) became the most beloved servant of Allah. He invited his Community (Ummah) to embrace these same exalted manners and said:

> ⟪quran 3:31⟫
> ⟪ar:‏قُلْ إِن كُنتُمْ تُحِبُّونَ ٱللَّهَ فَٱتَّبِعُونِى يُحْبِبْكُمُ ٱللَّهُ وَيَغْفِرْ لَكُمْ ذُنُوبَكُمْ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ غَفُورٌۭ رَّحِيمٌۭ ‎⟫
> *Say (O Muhammad PBUH to mankind): "If you (really) love Allah then follow me (i.e. accept Islamic Monotheism, follow the Quran and the Sunnah), Allah will love you and forgive you of your sins. And Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful."*

In explaining this, the Creator of the Universe declared: ⟪ar-quote:اول العقل التَوَدُّد⟫ "(The foundation of intellect is love)." That is, one must instill the principles of love within oneself and then express this love through outward action, so that it becomes the radiance of the exterior and the magnetism of the interior. To follow the Messenger of Allah is, in essence, to inculcate the moral character of the Messenger (upon him be peace and blessing) within oneself—for he alone achieved the perfection of these virtues. It is understood also that the connection of intellect with Truth (al-ḥaqq) is established through the finest conduct: gentleness, contentment, gratitude, generosity, modesty, wisdom, forbearance. These qualities are not merely beautiful in themselves; they also create love in both the outward and inward dimensions. A true intellect embodies all these virtues, for without them the intellect cannot attain completeness. Through these moral qualities, one creates depth in spontaneous action and brings forth the hidden treasures of the intellect. To mold all these virtues into the substance of one's own soul and unify them is, in a sense, an act of monotheism (tawḥīd). Though truth touches the soul, morality must serve as leaven for the intellect, so that it spreads in the form of action and reaches its perfection through the potentiality (bi-al-quwwah) becoming actuality (bi-al-fiʿl). All immorality is not merely evil but also a cause of intellectual debility. The heart becomes sick through vice, and the eye becomes blind—no longer able to perceive reality. The Qur'an says:

> ⟪quran 2:10⟫
> ⟪ar:‏فِى قُلُوبِهِم مَّرَضٌۭ فَزَادَهُمُ ٱللَّهُ مَرَضًۭا ۖ وَلَهُمْ عَذَابٌ أَلِيمٌۢ بِمَا كَانُوا۟ يَكْذِبُونَ ‎⟫
> *In their hearts is a disease (of doubt and hypocrisy) and Allah has increased their disease. A painful torment is theirs because they used to tell lies.*

> ⟪quran 22:46⟫
> ⟪ar:‏أَفَلَمْ يَسِيرُوا۟ فِى ٱلْأَرْضِ فَتَكُونَ لَهُمْ قُلُوبٌۭ يَعْقِلُونَ بِهَآ أَوْ آذَانٌۭ يَسْمَعُونَ بِهَا ۖ فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى ٱلْأَبْصَرُ وَلَكِن تَعْمَى ٱلْقُلُوبُ ٱلَّتِى فِى ٱلصُّدُورِ ‎⟫
> *Have they not travelled through the land, and have they hearts wherewith to understand and ears wherewith to hear? Verily, it is not the eyes that grow blind, but it is the hearts which are in the breasts that grow blind.*

Maulana Ali teaches that whoever masters his desires has prospered. Through this well-being comes the happiness born of certainty. And the spirit of this certainty flows from the union of intentionality and sincerity, coupled with observance of the Sacred Law. If sincerity is absent from one's deeds, then remains only hypocrisy—which itself is a form of idolatry (shirk). He says: "Know that even the slightest hypocrisy is idolatry." Allah clarifies this in the chapter:

> ⟪quran 107:1-7⟫
> ⟪ar:‏أَرَءَيْتَ ٱلَّذِى يُكَذِّبُ بِٱلدِّينِ ‎ ‏فَذَلِكَ ٱلَّذِى يَدُعُّ ٱلْيَتِيمَ ‎ ‏وَلَا يَحُضُّ عَلَىٰ طَعَامِ ٱلْمِسْكِينِ ‎ ‏فَوَيْلٌۭ لِّلْمُصَلِّينَ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِينَ هُمْ عَن صَلَاتِهِمْ سَاهُونَ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِينَ هُمْ يُرَآءُونَ ‎ ‏وَيَمْنَعُونَ ٱلْمَاعُونَ ‎⟫
> *Have you seen him who denies the Recompense? That is he who repulses the orphan (harshly), And urges not the feeding of AlMiskîn (the poor), So woe unto those performers of Salat (prayers) (hypocrites), Who delay their Salat (prayer) from their stated fixed times, Those who do good deeds only to be seen (of men), And refuse Al-Ma'un (small kindnesses e.g. salt, sugar, water, etc.).*

This chapter demonstrates that outward acts of worship hold no value unless wedded to sincerity and a moral life. Without this depth, religion becomes meaningless. An action grounded in pure intention is a true action; otherwise, all external deed is mere dust and does not ascend in the sight of Allah. As He says:

> ⟪quran 22:37⟫
> ⟪ar:‏لَن يَنَالَ ٱللَّهَ لُحُومُهَا وَلَا دِمَآؤُهَا وَلَكِن يَنَالُهُ ٱلتَّقْوَىٰ مِنكُمْ ۚ كَذَلِكَ سَخَّرَهَا لَكُمْ لِتُكَبِّرُوا۟ ٱللَّهَ عَلَىٰ مَا هَدَىٰكُمْ ۗ وَبَشِّرِ ٱلْمُحْسِنِينَ ‎⟫
> *It is neither their meat nor their blood that reaches Allah, but it is piety from you that reaches Him. Thus have We made them subject to you that you may magnify Allah for His Guidance to you. And give glad tidings (O Muhammad PBUH) to the Muhsinun (doers of good).*

Maulana Ali declares: he who lacks intellect has no religion. And the sleep of certainty is preferable to the wakefulness of doubt. Such persons remain spiritually alert even in slumber. Therefore the Messenger of Allah (upon him be peace and blessing) said: "My eyes sleep, but my heart remains awake." The wise person understands that, although outward acts of worship are necessary in themselves, the blessings of Allah cannot be obtained through them alone. There is required also an awareness of what one does and why. This consciousness, united with moral excellence and inner beauty, draws the acceptance of Allah. Maulana Ali says: "The best knowledge for you is that which renders your deeds acceptable to Allah."

*Verses referenced in this topic (curated linkage from KASHKOLE, TopicAyats—5 of 5 not already cited above):*

> ⟪quran 2:25⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَبَشِّرِ ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ وَعَمِلُوا۟ ٱلصَّلِحَتِ أَنَّ لَهُمْ جَنَّتٍۢ تَجْرِى مِن تَحْتِهَا ٱلْأَنْهَرُ ۖ كُلَّمَا رُزِقُوا۟ مِنْهَا مِن ثَمَرَةٍۢ رِّزْقًۭا ۙ قَالُوا۟ هَذَا ٱلَّذِى رُزِقْنَا مِن قَبْلُ ۖ وَأُتُوا۟ بِهِۦ مُتَشَبِهًۭا ۖ وَلَهُمْ فِيهَآ أَزْوَجٌۭ مُّطَهَّرَةٌۭ ۖ وَهُمْ فِيهَا خَلِدُونَ ‎⟫
> *And give glad tidings to those who believe and do righteous good deeds, that for them will be Gardens under which rivers flow (Paradise). Every time they will be provided with a fruit therefrom, they will say: "This is what we were provided with before," and they will be given things in resemblance (i.e. in the same form but different in taste) and they shall have therein Azwajun Mutahharatun (purified mates or wives), (having no menses, stools, urine, etc.) and they will abide therein forever.*

> ⟪quran 38:46⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّآ أَخْلَصْنَهُم بِخَالِصَةٍۢ ذِكْرَى ٱلدَّارِ ‎⟫
> *Verily, We did choose them by granting them (a good thing, i.e.) the remembrance of the home [in the Hereafter and they used to make the people remember it, and also they used to invite the people to obey Allah and to do good deeds for the Hereafter].*

> ⟪quran 55:60⟫
> ⟪ar:‏هَلْ جَزَآءُ ٱلْإِحْسَنِ إِلَّا ٱلْإِحْسَنُ ‎⟫
> *Is there any reward for good other than good?*

> ⟪quran 87:17⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱلْآخِرَةُ خَيْرٌۭ وَأَبْقَىٰٓ ‎⟫
> *Although the Hereafter is better and more lasting.*

> ⟪quran 89:27⟫
> ⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيَّتُهَا ٱلنَّفْسُ ٱلْمُطْمَئِنَّةُ ‎⟫
> *(It will be said to the pious): "O (you) the one in (complete) rest and satisfaction!*

<!-- section 6 (id=3600, raw_sort=6): دنیا میں رہتے ہوئے آخرت کی زندگی -->

## Life of the Hereafter While Dwelling in This World

He then declares that these chosen ones relate to the temporal world through their bodies alone; their souls are tethered to the Hereafter. This teaching can be illuminated by another statement of Amir al-Mu'minīn, in which he says he belongs to a group whose spiritual station is such that their hearts abide in Paradise while their bodies labor in this world. Your affirmation that the hearts are "settled" in heaven indicates that, though they remain alive in temporal existence, they have reached Paradise spiritually, conceptually, and in truth. This statement also confirms the saying of the Messenger of Allah (upon him be peace and blessing): that Allah preserves His servants in the state they themselves desire. Those who dwell in the upper realm anchor themselves in the Hereafter; by contrast, the temporal world becomes the source of all thought and imagination for those who dwell here. Their thinking, wishes, imaginings, reflections—all are imprisoned within the depths of this mortal sphere. Every deed they perform bears the seal of this lower realm. The wise, by contrast, base every action upon the reward they shall receive in the Hereafter, understanding the truth that every action is dust save that which flows from sincerity and which Allah the Almighty accepts.

Maulana Ali frequently mentions the blessings of Paradise and the torments of Hell in his sermons. Does this not, then, impart a commercial character to the divine commands? Not at all. There is a vast distance between knowledge of Paradise and Hell, and the correct understanding of them. His aim is to awaken in the Community a feeling through which moral character may be illuminated. Otherwise, from a mercantile perspective, an object becomes worthless once the purpose of the transaction is fulfilled. Will the virtues of piety, noble character, and forbidden vice become useless upon entering Paradise? Never. Rather, the very atmosphere of Paradise shall consist of these attributes themselves. His mention of Paradise serves this purpose: that a person prepare himself for the environment of Paradise while still in this world, so that he may enter it prepared. In one of his sermons he speaks of Paradise and says:

If you turn your heart to the descriptions of Paradise, your soul will be disgusted with the wonders, desires, pleasures and charming scenery of this world. And she will be lost in the scene of Paradise, with swaying streams on the banks of the canal, with its roots in the dunes of fragrant musk, and from whose branches hang glittering pearls.

The highest degree of certainty—⟪ar:'روحانیت'⟫ (rūḥāniyyat, spirituality)—transcends both the desire for Paradise and fear of Hell. Yet when this level of spirituality is attained, the soul becomes "heavenly" even while traversing this mortal existence. The state of Paradise is such that the soul finds satisfaction only in the mighty presence of its Lord and craves nothing else. This condition the Qur'an names ⟪ar:'نفس المطمئنۃ'⟫ (nafs al-muṭmā'inna, the tranquil soul):

> ⟪quran 89:27⟫
> ⟪ar:‏يَٓأَيَّتُهَا ٱلنَّفْسُ ٱلْمُطْمَئِنَّةُ ‎⟫
> *(It will be said to the pious): "O (you) the one in (complete) rest and satisfaction!*

The satisfaction of this soul arises from complete faith. This verse echoes throughout Maulana Ali's sermons:

> ⟪quran 87:17⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱلْآخِرَةُ خَيْرٌۭ وَأَبْقَىٰٓ ‎⟫
> *Although the Hereafter is better and more lasting.*

The Hereafter is not merely a "time" when divine revelations shall be fulfilled, but a "dwelling-place" where all supreme, terrestrial, and divine attributes shall manifest—the faintest reflection of which appears in this world. In this capacity, the very mention and conception of Paradise leads to purification of the intellect and estrangement from temporal pleasures and desires, as Allah states:

> ⟪quran 38:46⟫
> ⟪ar:‏إِنَّآ أَخْلَصْنَهُم بِخَالِصَةٍۢ ذِكْرَى ٱلدَّارِ ‎⟫
> *Verily, We did choose them by granting them (a good thing, i.e.) the remembrance of the home [in the Hereafter and they used to make the people remember it, and also they used to invite the people to obey Allah and to do good deeds for the Hereafter].*

In other words, pure knowledge of the Hereafter engenders the concept of becoming a "paradise" in this world. This allows a person to perform the righteous deeds and moral virtues that exist in their primordial form in the Hereafter, and thus to attain happiness both here and there. It is thus proven that these moral qualities can only arise in a person whose eyes the veil of this world has been lifted from—who can see and feel the life of the Hereafter while inhabiting this mortal realm. In truth, goodness and excellence are not performed so that reward shall come in the Hereafter; rather, goodness itself is part of that reward. Allah says:

> ⟪quran 55:60⟫
> ⟪ar:‏هَلْ جَزَآءُ ٱلْإِحْسَنِ إِلَّا ٱلْإِحْسَنُ ‎⟫
> *Is there any reward for good other than good?*

Maulana Ali teaches that the fruit of any good deed resembles its root—meaning that the end of any action is rooted in its beginning (intention). At this juncture the following Qur'anic verse becomes especially pertinent:

> ⟪quran 2:25⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَبَشِّرِ ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ وَعَمِلُوا۟ ٱلصَّلِحَتِ أَنَّ لَهُمْ جَنَّتٍۢ تَجْرِى مِن تَحْتِهَا ٱلْأَنْهَرُ ۖ كُلَّمَا رُزِقُوا۟ مِنْهَا مِن ثَمَرَةٍۢ رِّزْقًۭا ۙ قَالُوا۟ هَذَا ٱلَّذِى رُزِقْنَا مِن قَبْلُ ۖ وَأُتُوا۟ بِهِۦ مُتَشَبِهًۭا ۖ وَلَهُمْ فِيهَآ أَزْوَجٌۭ مُّطَهَّرَةٌۭ ۖ وَهُمْ فِيهَا خَلِدُونَ ‎⟫
> *And give glad tidings to those who believe and do righteous good deeds, that for them will be Gardens under which rivers flow (Paradise). Every time they will be provided with a fruit therefrom, they will say: "This is what we were provided with before," and they will be given things in resemblance (i.e. in the same form but different in taste) and they shall have therein Azwajun Mutahharatun (purified mates or wives), (having no menses, stools, urine, etc.) and they will abide therein forever.*

The fruits of the Hereafter are tasted in this world through all forms of goodness, beauty, truth, and the joys that spring from them. All these flavors are of the authentic nourishment of the Hereafter, which shall be obtained in Paradise. In the realm of ethics, righteous deeds and the very sense of the imperative for goodness (iḥsān, benevolence) are necessary not only for salvation but for the liberation of this life. They emancipate the soul outwardly from the bondage of sins and inwardly from the bondage of ego. This realization has been the focus and purpose of all his teachings.

<!-- section 7 (id=3581, raw_sort=7): وہ چیز جو آسان بھی ہے اور مشکل بھی -->

## That Which Is Both Easy and Difficult

He declared: "Those things are easy for them and difficult for those who are extravagant." They—the pious—love that which alienates the heedless. This saying captures an effect that perpetually marks the hearts of Allah's saints. They continually remember their death and their meeting with the Lord, and through His aid they renounce the world. What appears strange and forbidding to the heedless are the apparent trials, sufferings, and deprivations of temporal life. The Qur'an speaks of them thus:

> ⟪quran 2:155-157⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَنَبْلُوَنَّكُم بِشَىْءٍۢ مِّنَ ٱلْخَوْفِ وَٱلْجُوعِ وَنَقْصٍۢ مِّنَ ٱلْأَمْوَلِ وَٱلْأَنفُسِ وَٱلثَّمَرَتِ ۗ وَبَشِّرِ ٱلصَّبِرِينَ ‎ ‏ٱلَّذِينَ إِذَآ أَصَبَتْهُم مُّصِيبَةٌۭ قَالُوٓا۟ إِنَّا لِلَّهِ وَإِنَّآ إِلَيْهِ رَجِعُونَ ‎ ‏أُو۟لَٓئِكَ عَلَيْهِمْ صَلَوَتٌۭ مِّن رَّبِّهِمْ وَرَحْمَةٌۭ ۖ وَأُو۟لَٓئِكَ هُمُ ٱلْمُهْتَدُونَ ‎⟫
> *And certainly, We shall test you with something of fear, hunger, loss of wealth, lives and fruits, but give glad tidings to As-Sabirin (the patient ones, etc.). Who, when afflicted with calamity, say: "Truly! To Allah we belong and truly, to Him we shall return." They are those on whom are the Salawat (i.e. blessings, etc.) (i.e. who are blessed and will be forgiven) from their Lord, and (they are those who) receive His Mercy, and it is they who are the guided-ones.*

These ⟪ar:مھتدون⟫ (muhtadūn, the guided) are patient amid adversity and perceive the blessings that arise from such patience through the clarity of their knowledge:

> ⟪quran 103:1-3⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَٱلْعَصْرِ ‎ ‏إِنَّ ٱلْإِنسَنَ لَفِى خُسْرٍ ‎ ‏إِلَّا ٱلَّذِينَ آمَنُوا۟ وَعَمِلُوا۟ ٱلصَّلِحَتِ وَتَوَاصَوْا۟ بِٱلْحَقِّ وَتَوَاصَوْا۟ بِٱلصَّبْرِ ‎⟫
> *By Al-'Asr (the time). Verily! Man is in loss, Except those who believe (in Islamic Monotheism) and do righteous good deeds, and recommend one another to the truth (i.e. order one another to perform all kinds of good deeds (Al-Ma'which Allah has ordained, and abstain from all kinds of sins and evil deeds (Al-Munkar which Allah has forbidden), and recommend one another to patience (for the sufferings, harms, and injuries which one may encounter in Allah's Cause during preaching His religion of Islamic Monotheism or Jihad, etc.).*

These are the believers, steadfast in trial, who know their source and their destiny and who trust in Allah's word: "To Him we shall return." Through this, the afflictions of the world become lighter for them. The heedless, by contrast, experience only the bitterness and anguish of suffering. The relief within that suffering remains hidden from him by ignorance, yet is manifest to the wise:

> ⟪quran 94:5-6⟫
> ⟪ar:‏فَإِنَّ مَعَ ٱلْعُسْرِ يُسْرًا ‎ ‏إِنَّ مَعَ ٱلْعُسْرِ يُسْرًۭا ‎⟫
> *So verily, with the hardship, there is relief, Verily, with the hardship, there is relief (i.e. there is one hardship with two reliefs, so one hardship cannot overcome two reliefs).*

These wise ones not only perceive the fruit of ease that follows hardship through their insight; they taste it directly. They take suffering and trials as their companions, while the ignorant become estranged from them. For this reason he said: "Death is as dear to me as a mother's breast is to a newborn child."[^cite-7] And you speak of this love of death while yet living. It is as though you see the reality of death and the blessings that come after it with your own eyes. He also declared: "I am the death of death!" For temporal life is itself a kind of death—a hindrance and constraint. Allah says:

> ⟪quran 29:64⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَمَا هَذِهِ ٱلْحَيَوٰةُ ٱلدُّنْيَآ إِلَّا لَهْوٌۭ وَلَعِبٌۭ ۚ وَإِنَّ ٱلدَّارَ ٱلْآخِرَةَ لَهِىَ ٱلْحَيَوَانُ ۚ لَوْ كَانُوا۟ يَعْلَمُونَ ‎⟫
> *And this life of the world is only amusement and play! Verily, the home of the Hereafter, that is the life indeed (i.e. the eternal life that will never end), if they but knew*

### Asceticism

It must be emphasized here that the purpose of these utterances is not the renunciation of the physical world itself, but the renunciation of "worldliness"—the attachment of self to temporal things. That is, one must overcome the bond of the ⟪ar:'أنا'⟫ (anā, ego or "I") that clings to the world. He describes this state, following the ⟪ar:نہج البلاغۃ⟫ (Nahj al-Balāghah, the Path of Eloquence), as ⟪ar:'زہد'⟫ (zuhd, asceticism):

This world is a place for which destruction has been ordered, and destruction has been decreed for its people. This world is sweet and attractive. He who goes out in search of him comes running to him; he who looks at him settles in his heart. Therefore, gather the best of provisions from here and depart, and do not seek anything beyond what is most essential.

This is the finest provision! In another place he stated that the best provision ⟪ar:'زہد'⟫ (zuhd) for the next life is ⟪ar:'تقوی'⟫ (taqwā, God-consciousness)—obtained to the degree that a person is indifferent to the perishable things of this world.

> ⟪quran 17:18⟫
> ⟪ar:‏مَّن كَانَ يُرِيدُ ٱلْعَاجِلَةَ عَجَّلْنَا لَهُۥ فِيهَا مَا نَشَآءُ لِمَن نُّرِيدُ ثُمَّ جَعَلْنَا لَهُۥ جَهَنَّمَ يَصْلَىٰهَا مَذْمُومًۭا مَّدْحُورًۭا ‎⟫
> *Whoever wishes for the quick-passing (transitory enjoyment of this world), We readily grant him what We will for whom We like. Then, afterwards, We have appointed for him Hell, he will burn therein disgraced and rejected, (far away from Allah's Mercy).*

The sayings of Maulana Ali fully embody this contempt for the temporal:

I swear by Allah! In my eyes, this world of yours is as despicable as a pig's bone chewed in the hand of a whip. The example of the world is that of a snake that is soft to the touch, but filled with deadly venom. For me, this world is the same as the discharge flowing from a goat's nose.

The greatness and majesty of Allah render this world contemptible and lowly. When the world is viewed in the light of the Absolute Real (al-Ḥaqq), all its pleasures appear fleeting and trivial. The insignificance of His servants compared to Allah's magnificence becomes evident. Maulana Ali praised a believer, saying: ⟪ar-quote:کان یعظمہ فی عینی، صغار الدنیا فی عینہ⟫ "(His contempt for the world made him great in my eyes)."[^cite-8] This world was not considered base because it was useless, but because of the majesty of Allah, the reality of the Hereafter, and the absolute truths which stood before their eyes. On account of these, the world became insignificant to them. For:

> ⟪quran 28:88⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَلَا تَدْعُ مَعَ ٱللَّهِ إِلَهًا آخَرَ ۘ لَآ إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ ۚ كُلُّ شَىْءٍ هَالِكٌ إِلَّا وَجْهَهُۥ ۚ لَهُ ٱلْحُكْمُ وَإِلَيْهِ تُرْجَعُونَ ‎⟫
> *And invoke not any other ilah (god) along with Allah, La ilaha illa Huwa (none has the right to be worshipped but He). Everything will perish save His Face. His is the Decision, and to Him you (all) shall be returned.*

The value of this world lies in that it contains creatures of Allah who praise Him morning and evening and acknowledge His grandeur:

> ⟪quran 17:44⟫
> ⟪ar:‏تُسَبِّحُ لَهُ ٱلسَّمَوَتُ ٱلسَّبْعُ وَٱلْأَرْضُ وَمَن فِيهِنَّ ۚ وَإِن مِّن شَىْءٍ إِلَّا يُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِهِۦ وَلَكِن لَّا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ۗ إِنَّهُۥ كَانَ حَلِيمًا غَفُورًۭا ‎⟫
> *The seven heavens and the earth and all that is therein, glorify Him and there is not a thing but glorifies His Praise. But you understand not their glorification. Truly, He is Ever Forbearing, Oft-Forgiving.*

> ⟪quran 24:41⟫
> ⟪ar:‏أَلَمْ تَرَ أَنَّ ٱللَّهَ يُسَبِّحُ لَهُۥ مَن فِى ٱلسَّمَوَتِ وَٱلْأَرْضِ وَٱلطَّيْرُ صَٓفَّتٍۢ ۖ كُلٌّۭ قَدْ عَلِمَ صَلَاتَهُۥ وَتَسْبِيحَهُۥ ۗ وَٱللَّهُ عَلِيمٌۢ بِمَا يَفْعَلُونَ ‎⟫
> *See you not (O Muhammad PBUH) that Allah, He it is Whom glorify whosoever is in the heavens and the earth, and the birds with wings out-spread (in their flight). Of each one He (Allah) knows indeed his Salat (prayer) and his glorification, [or everyone knows his Salat (prayer) and his glorification], and Allah is All-Aware of what they do.*

Here arises a certain paradox: the world is, in one sense, a "bad place," yet in another it is a "good place" where one may prepare all deeds for the Hereafter. In his sermons, he praises Allah for the creation and industry of nature—for peacocks, birds, even ants, dragons, and elephants. In another sermon, he uses the blessings of this world to illuminate the blessings of the Hereafter, saying that when the eye beholds the scenes of Paradise, even the finest blessings, the most beautiful vistas, the most delicious pleasures of this world become despised in the sight of mankind. And because of this perception, all the trials of this world become light to man and even a source of gladness. ⟪ar:زاہدین،⟫ With these spiritual resources obtained through faith, the ascetics (zāhidīn) endure all hardships. They possess a knowledge that grants them such firm and perfect certainty that they declare: ⟪ar-quote:لو کشف الغطاء ما الزدت یقینا⟫ "Even if the veil were lifted, my certainty would not increase."[^cite-9]

Speaking of his sincerity, he says: "I bear witness that there is no god but Allah! The sincerity of this testimony has been tested, and its essence is mingled with certainty." In Arabic, the word ⟪ar:'مصاص'⟫ (misās) means to suck or extract, and the subtle part of any object is that which contains its essence—just as the juice of fruit is the part one sucks from it. The sincerity that has been tested is the spiritual substance of the soul itself, not imaginative ideas arising from words or apparent meanings. Words are merely an echo of the spiritual substance established in the heart.

You return to his saying that those things are easy for them which are difficult for those estranged from truth. They love that which alienates the ignorant. In one sense, it is "suffering," and in another it is "death" itself. For the heedless, death is a terrible dissolution in which he is separated from his body—for him the end of life rather than its beginning. It is separation from that which has been his focus and center throughout his life. For the wise, by contrast, death is salvation from suffering and a meeting with Allah; it is the consummation of monotheism (tawḥīd), where he merges into the transcendent realm and becomes "one" with Him. Only he who immerses himself in this ocean can taste its pleasure. The radiance of this whole consumes all components to ash. Those who remain enslaved to ego are caught in fragmentation, outward and inward. The wise person, by contrast, is forever absorbed in the remembrance of the Absolute Reality; the joy arising from it displaces his ego and shatters all illusions.

<!-- section 8 (id=3583, raw_sort=8): عدل کا فلسفہ -->

## The Philosophy of Justice

The finest example of Amir al-Mu'minīn ⟪ar:أمیر المومنین⟫'s philosophy of ⟪ar:'عدل'⟫ (ʿadl, justice) is found in the letter he wrote to his governor and military commander ⟪ar:'مالک اشتر بن الحارث النخئی'⟫ (Mālik al-Ashtar ibn al-Ḥārith al-Nakhʿī) when appointing him governor of Egypt. In his view, the inclination of moral laws toward justice pertains not only to the external world but also to the spiritual realm. The principles of justice he outlines transcend the limits of mere process. In his teaching, thought and action emerge as complementary and intimately related, reflecting the message of ⟪ar:'توحید'⟫ (tawḥīd, monotheism—the acknowledgment of divine unity). The lives of the Prophet (upon him be peace and blessing) and Amir al-Mu'minīn exemplify these principles.

There are two aspects to consider: First, that Amir al-Mu'minīn ⟪ar:امیر المونین⟫ was himself a perfect embodiment of justice. Second, in the foundations of his justice we discover profound spiritual lessons—how a life spent in love of God naturally leads to a righteous life, a life that mirrors itself in ⟪ar:'الحق'⟫ (al-Ḥaqq, the Truth). If we examine ethics and ⟪ar:'أحسان'⟫ (iḥsān, excellence) in the broad field of morality, we find that the integration of the dimensions and nuances of morals and ethics creates a depth that molds both into a fundamental realm. Ordinary questions of right and wrong are transformed into a singular question: "What is Reality?"

Ali's spiritual understanding of monotheism is not merely that there is no peer to Allah, but that nothing stands in opposition to Him. For this reason, every good ⟪ar:حَسَنَ⟫ (ḥasan) deed—that is, a deed pleasing to Allah—is not the opposite of moral evil in the conventional sense. It possesses an individual and singular existence of its own, in contrast to which everything else is only ⟪ar:'عدم'⟫ (ʿadam, non-being or nothingness). Thus, if good ⟪ar:الحق⟫ (al-Ḥaqq) belongs to the positive dimension, then evil—⟪ar:الحق⟫—represents the "capacity" or "inclination" to negate good. In other words: if goodness is an attribute of the Creator, evil is an attribute of creation which He has brought into being. Allah declares:

> ⟪quran 113:2⟫
> ⟪ar:‏مِن شَرِّ مَا خَلَقَ ‎⟫
> *"From the evil of what He has created;*

This verse is often mistranslated as "the evil that Allah has created," which is both religiously and linguistically incorrect. Allah is explicitly stating that ⟪ar:‏مِن شَرِّ مَا خَلَقَ،⟫ the "evil" emanating from His "creation" is what must be guarded against. That is: Allah created free creatures endowed with good qualities. They themselves brought "evil" into being through the negation of those attributes. Therefore, it cannot be said that God created evil; He created only those conditions in which His creatures chose to participate, even though they could have chosen Truth. This proves that evil is, in fact, merely a negation of good and does not possess existence in itself. The verse may also be understood thus: I seek refuge in the "Truth" from the "unjust." Evil appears only as a negation of Truth, which the eye of the heart perceives as an "illusion." And just as every illusion may be shattered by reason, so do Truth and goodness possess the power to overcome any evil. The more a soul is sincere before its Lord, the more capable it becomes of overcoming evil through the firmness of its will. This maturity extends from intellect to the heart, which convinces it that good is more "right" than evil—conviction strengthened ⟪ar:'الحق'⟫ by direct knowledge. Since evil does not exist in itself, but is a deficiency departed from its proper place, it requires correction and return to Truth. For this reason, in the vision of Amir al-Mu'minīn, the fulfillment of one's obligations and the discharge of rights toward all—Allah, oneself, and all creation—constitutes the definition of justice. Referring to this, he says: "Justice is the act of keeping things in their proper place." The more a person's actions accord with their rightful station, the more just he will be; and this essence of justice appears in a person to the degree it relates him to ⟪ar:'الحق'⟫ (al-Ḥaqq). First and foremost, a person must become "good" (ḥasan); only then will he be capable of performing "good," and to become good is to achieve ⟪ar:توحید⟫ (tawḥīd), monotheism, because it is He alone who is the Truth!

An analysis of the letter Amir al-Mu'minīn wrote to ⟪ar:مالک اشتر⟫ (Mālik al-Ashtar) reveals that justice is not the name of any single attribute, but rather the sum and integration of all virtues—especially the three cardinal attributes of temperance, courage, and wisdom. When these three virtues occupy their proper places, then this self may be called just.

Ethics and spirituality are woven together, as evidenced by the narration of when the Messenger of Allah (upon him be peace and blessing) sat with his companions and Gabriel (peace be upon him) came to him in human form and asked: ⟪ar:یا محمد (ﷺ)!⟫ "Tell me about Islam." The Prophet replied: "⟪ar:الأسلام⟫ Islam is to bear witness that there is no god but Allah, and that Muhammad is His Messenger, and to establish prayer, give zakat, fast the month of Ramaḍān, and perform pilgrimage to the House of Allah." Gabriel said: "You have spoken truth!" He then asked: "Tell me about ⟪ar:'ایمان'⟫ (īmān, faith)." The Prophet said: "Faith is to believe in Allah, His Angels, His Book, His Messengers, the Last Day, and in divine decree and providence." Gabriel said again: "You have spoken truth!" Then he asked: "Tell me what ⟪ar:'أحسان'⟫ (iḥsān, excellence) is." The Prophet answered: "It is to worship Allah as if you see Him with your own eyes; and if you cannot achieve this, then [worship Him in the awareness] that surely He sees you."[^cite-10]

The constant awareness of Allah's presence and the consciousness of standing before His sight is common to all these sublime stations. As the Prophet (upon him be peace and blessing) declared: "Worship Allah as though you see Him." Therefore, the concept of excellence (iḥsān) is rooted in Islam itself. On one hand, Amir al-Mu'minīn states in his sermons: "Know that every action you perform depends upon your niyyah (intention)—that is, upon how you regard ⟪ar:'الحق'⟫ (the Truth)." On the other hand, he warns: "Hypocrisy, arrogance, and self-praise are the finest snares of Satan by which he destroys the ⟪ar:محسنین⟫ (muḥsinīn, those who do good)." The fundamental purpose of worship is the dissolution of one's ego and self in the sight of Allah. His first statement calls for justice, which places all things—including oneself—in their rightful position. His second statement illuminates the root cause of all injustices: ⟪ar:کبر⟫ (kibar, arrogance) and ⟪ar:حرس⟫ (ḥirs, avarice) and ⟪ar:حسد⟫ (ḥasad, envy), which are called the ⟪ar:أمہات معاصی⟫ (ummahāt al-maʿāsī, "mothers" or root causes of sin).


<!-- section 9 (id=3582, raw_sort=9): Letter to Malik al-Ashtar -->

## Letter to Malik al-Ashtar

This is what Allah's servant ʿAlī, Amīr al-Muʾminīn, has ordered Malik ibn al-Ḥārith al-Ashtar in his instrument of appointment when he made him Governor of Egypt—for the collection of its revenues, the waging of jihad against its enemies, the betterment of its people, and the prosperity of its cities.

⟪ar-quote:هذَا مَا أَمَرَ بِهِ عَبْدُ اللهِ عَلِيٌ أَميِرُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ، مَالِكَ بْنَ الْحَارِثِ الاْشْتَرَ فِي عَهْدِهِ إِلَيْهِ، حِينَ وَلاَّهُ مِصْرَ- جِبْوةَ خَرَاجِهَا، وَجِهَادَ عَدُوِّهَا، وَاسْتِصْلاَحَ أَهْلِهَا، وَعِمَارَةِ بِلاَدِهَا⟫

He has ordered him to fear Allah—to prefer obedience to Him—and to follow what He has commanded in His Book out of His obligatory and elective ordinances. Without adherence to these, no one achieves felicity; conversely, one incurs wretchedness only through denial and neglect of them. He is commanded to aid Allah the Glorified with his heart, hand, and tongue. For Allah, whose name is Sublime, assumes responsibility for aiding those who aid Him and exalting those who exalt Him. He also orders him to break his heart away from passions and to restrain it at the moment of their surge; for the soul (*nafs*, the self) commands toward evil unless Allah extends mercy.[^cite-11]
