I am a companion of the rays of Allah's divine glory! I am the first, I am the last, I am the outward, I am the inward! I am the Face of God! I am the mirror of God, His pen and His tablet! I am He whom the Gospels call Elijah! I am the keeper of the Messenger of Allah's mysteries!"

Though such words may suggest *shirk* to the outward observer, those of inner knowing comprehend their true meaning. Only if we grasp the first statement—"I am a sign of Allah the Almighty!"—do all others become clear. In this sermon, Mawlā ʿAlī identifies himself with Allah's knowledge, not with human knowledge of Allah. The knowledge Allah possesses of Himself is not the knowledge humans possess of Allah. Knowledge, knower, and known become one when they reach this supreme knowledge, just as knowledge and existence become one at consciousness's pinnacle. At this point, the knower becomes self-aware: knowledge becomes consciousness that the Real has of Itself. Consciousness accesses this knowledge by destroying every particle of its existence in Allah. One must forget oneself to remember Allah. To become Allah's knowledge, everything must become what Allah Himself is. Then humanity becomes a ray of divine greatness. All mysteries—*al-sirr* (the Secret)—are hidden in the Ultimate Secret, the Essence of Allah, which encompasses all Names within itself. Thus does one say: "I am the first, I am the last, I am the outward, I am the inward."

Then you call yourself the Face of God. This may be understood through the mirror analogy. When a person gazes upon a mirror, the mirror ceases to exist in perception. All the viewer's attention falls upon the reflected face. The mirror's existence goes unnoticed. That is, the mirror loses its identity. Conversely, when attention focuses on the mirror, one cannot see the reflection. At this moment, the mirror identifies with the form the eye beholds. Now both mirror and form become one, though they remain two separate objects simultaneously. So long as attention dwells on the mirror, it veils the face; but as soon as gaze turns to the face, the veil lifts, revealing obedience. The mirror's negation of existence mirrors the negation of humanity before Allah's divinity, while focus on the mirror's image mirrors remembrance of Allah.

Here again we observe paradox. Those whom Allah loves, who correctly interpret His names and attributes—these know that those attributes are not their own. This is why the saints and the *aṣfiyāʾ* (the Pure Ones) represent Allah's Beautiful Names. The light of these attributes shines through them because they have consumed their hearts' mirrors with remembrance of Allah, erasing all egoistic rust. Physicality cannot obstruct the reflection of this light through them. These beings—whose hearts dwell in heaven while their bodies labor in this world—live as though already dead to this life. Allah has said:

> ⟪quran 29:64⟫
> ⟪ar:‏وَمَا هَذِهِ ٱلْحَيَوٰةُ ٱلدُّنْيَآ إِلَّا لَهْوٌۭ وَلَعِبٌۭ ۚ وَإِنَّ ٱلدَّارَ ٱلْآخِرَةَ لَهِىَ ٱلْحَيَوَانُ ۚ لَوْ كَانُوا۟ يَعْلَمُونَ ‎⟫
> *And this life of the world is only amusement and play! Verily, the home of the Hereafter, that is the life indeed (i.e. the eternal life that will never end), if they but knew*
> — **Quran 29:64**

Therefore, their instincts remain ever united with divine realities. When the sword of Ibn Muljam struck Mawlā ʿAlī, he cried instinctively: "I have succeeded by the Lord of the Kaʿbah!" In the moment of his death, naught existed but remembrance of Allah. Upon his tongue was no god but Allah. He departed this life on the same principle by which he lived it. He fulfilled the teaching of the Messenger of Allah: "The most beautiful death is when one passes away with one's tongue moist with the remembrance of Allah."


<!-- section 24 (id=5722, raw_sort=24): The Spirit Of The Intellect -->

## The Spirit Of The Intellect

Our purpose here is to outline certain dimensions of the spiritual ethos of Maulana Ali, with particular emphasis upon its intellectual character. We aim to demonstrate the pivotal role exercised by the intellect (*al-ʿAql*, the faculty of direct contemplative apprehension) in the articulation and assimilation of Maulana Ali's spiritual teachings. Central to this enquiry is the concept of the "spirit of the intellect"—a spirit that not only comprises the operations of rational thought but transcends them, embracing domains remote from what modernity associates with intelligence: moral conduct, aesthetic perception, and the deepest capacities of human awareness. We shall show how this spiritual understanding of the intellect relates intimately to the Quranic concept of the spirit (*al-Rūḥ*), and we shall explore the fruits of engaging profoundly with this unifying spirit proper to the intellect itself.

At the outset, a terminological distinction serves our purpose. We render *ʿAql* as "intellect" rather than "reason," thereby invoking the original sense of the Latin *intellectus*: that faculty capable of direct, contemplative vision of transcendent realities. Reason, by contrast, operates indirectly and discursively—it thinks *about* realities through logical operations and arrives at mental concepts. Through intellect, one may contemplate or "see" the Absolute; through reason alone, one can only think about it.[^cite-50]

Three factors prove essential to the spiritual intellectual perspective we examine here: the centrality of revelation, the harmony between revelation and intellect, and the distinction between intellect (as the principle articulating consciousness) and reason.

First, revelation stands at the centre of Maulana Ali's life and teachings. Both his thought and his conduct may be read as a spiritual commentary—a *taʾwīl* (esoteric exegesis)—upon the sacred text. Doctrinally, Maulana Ali possessed the innate aptitude for performing *taʾwīl*. Existentially, his life unfolded as a dramatic manifestation of the inner meaning of revelation: from his birth within the sacred Kaaba to his martyrdom in the mosque of Kufa. His refusal to remain at the superficial surface of things—whether in religion, ethics, or politics—embodied an unflinching fidelity to the sacred substance of faith. This fidelity required both intellectual penetration inward and combative resistance outward. The Prophet's saying holds profound significance in this connection: "Ali will fight for the *taʾwīl* of the Qur'an as I fought for its *tanzīl* (literal revelation)."[^cite-51]

The three civil wars that dominated Maulana Ali's caliphate were fought, it may justly be argued, for the sake of the correct interpretation and application of the Qur'an—for the clarification of its fundamental message (*tawḥīd*, divine oneness) and the realization of this unity across all spiritual and social dimensions. This claim finds vivid substantiation in Maulana Ali's response to a bedouin who, as the Battle of the Camel (*al-Jamal*) was about to commence, approached him and asked: "O Commander of the Faithful! Do you say that Allah is one?" Those present remonstrated with the bedouin, deeming such inquiry ill-timed. Yet Maulana Ali intervened: "Leave him, for surely what the bedouin wishes is what we wish for the people."

This response illuminates a principle of cardinal importance: right intention takes precedence over outward action, however significant that action may be. For Maulana Ali, the most fundamental intention motivating every deed has been the knowledge of the true nature of ultimate reality. Whether the context be religious observance or military engagement, what Maulana Ali "wishes for the people" is this knowledge—knowledge that must lie at the very heart of all concerns, motivations, and acts, whether on the battlefield or the prayer rug. Thus there exists no moment, no place, where the question "What do you mean by saying Allah is one?" is irrelevant or misplaced. He fought in the first instance for the sake of correct knowledge of the Divine, and in fulfillment of the Prophet's prophecy.[^cite-52]

Upon the very field of battle, Maulana Ali discoursed upon the meaning of divine oneness. The opening of his reply contains a key to his entire spiritual ethos. He refutes the notion that Allah's oneness can be understood as a numerical or countable oneness. He tells the bedouin: "It is not permissible to say 'one' while holding in mind any numerical conception, for 'that which has no second does not enter into the category of numbers.'"[^cite-53]

"That which has no second" signifies not merely a divinity to which no partner may be ascribed. Rather, it denotes that Reality which possesses no "otherness"—the Real (*al-Ḥaqq*), both transcendent and immanent, beyond all "things" yet manifested through all "things," at once inaccessible and inseparable. In these words, Maulana Ali effects a shift of consciousness (for those with "ears to hear"), moving from dogmatic assertion of one God against many gods to the mystical apprehension of the one and only Reality, before which all else appears as mere appearance. All things become perceivable as outward phenomena calling out to be deciphered. They emerge as signs (*āyāt*) pointing to that which they manifest. To retrace these appearances back to their source and origin is the literal meaning of *taʾwīl*: returning to the *Awwal* (the First). The Real is described as the beginning and end of all things, the manifest and the hidden; nothing in existence remains apart from this one Reality:

> ⟪quran 57:3⟫  
> He is the First and the Last, the Outward and the Inward, and He has knowledge of all things.

On the other hand, apparent multiplicity must be "brought back" to true unity. As Allah declares:

> ⟪quran 41:53⟫  
> We will show them Our Signs in the universe, and in their ownselves, until it becomes manifest to them that this is the truth.

In a saying attributed to Maulana Ali, the Prophet is referenced as the first of those whom Allah taught the science of *taʾwīl*. The Prophet thereafter taught Maulana Ali, and according to the Prophet himself: "Truly, Ali is with the Qur'an, and the Qur'an is with Ali." In another celebrated saying, Maulana Ali claimed he could load seventy camels with pages of commentary on Surah al-Fatiḥa. His cousin Ibn Abbas declared he had learned the science of interpretation from Ali.

Fundamental to this matter is the saying of Maulana Ali: "The Qur'an consists of a book inscribed between two covers; it speaks not with a tongue and cannot do without an interpreter (⟪ar:لا بُدَّا مِنَ التَّرجُمٰن⟫)."[^cite-54] This exchange between revelation and intellect finds further emphasis in another saying: "The intellect of a man is the interpreter of his mind (⟪ar:رَاسُ الرَّجَل تَرجُمان عَقلِہ⟫)." Elsewhere, he states explicitly that the intellect is "the messenger of the Real."

Thus Maulana Ali's teachings, which express his spiritual ethos, are to be read essentially as creative interpretations of Quranic revelation. They inaugurate a movement from the letters of revelation to its spirit, and from the spirit of revelation to the spirit of the Revealer:

> Letters of revelation → Spirit of Revelation → Revealer (*al-Ḥaqq*)

This process of "retracing" or returning to the source, the origin, the beginning (*al-awwal*), is accomplished insofar as the spirit of the intellect is enlivened within the interpreter. Yet the Revealer remains necessarily concealed (as the essence of divine Reality), even as He is revealed through revelation. Divine Reality in itself is too great for words. Only the forms of its manifestations are susceptible to disclosure. This dialectical interplay between form and formlessness, the evident and the hidden, the revealed and the concealed—all such antinomies lie at the root of the paradoxical mode of expression for which Maulana Ali is renowned.

One of the most striking aspects of Maulana Ali's perspective is its revelation of the hollowness underlying many false or excessively polarized dichotomies that would later characterize—or be imposed upon—Islamic thought. The dichotomy between intellect and revelation manifests in various polarized ramifications: reason versus faith, convention (*sunna*) versus nature (*ṭabīʿa*), imitation (*taqlīd*) versus verification (*taḥqīq*), piety (*taqwá*) versus inquiry. Yet in Maulana Ali's ethos, intellect and revelation are complementary, and at the highest level, inseparable, since truth is one. While the human intellect cannot be equated with divine revelation, the spirit animating the intellect is not constrained by human limitation. It is this spirit which perceives and grasps the divine quality of revelation.

The divine spirit articulating the form and infusing the content of revelation must be, in some degree, intelligible to human consciousness. Were this not so, revelation would reduce to mere rules and regulations for the will rather than a source of creative inspiration for the intellect. It cannot be overstressed: divine revelation presupposes creative engagement with all the intellect's resources, not the suppression of reason and the imposition of unintelligible decree. This is what the Qur'an itself repeatedly calls for.

The revealed text is silent, "speaking" only through the "interpreter"—the intellect. This understanding rigorously excludes all literalism and superficiality. It excludes all attempts to confine meaning within a flat, unilateral reading. Rather, such an understanding seeks the opposite: to "dis-close" rather than "enclose" meaning, to open the inner horizons of multiple strata of significance hidden in the text. This multi-layered significance is plumbed in accordance with the depth and penetration of the interpreter's intellect. This "prophetic" intellect rejoins the source of revelation itself, for it is this "inner prophet," or inmost degree of consciousness, that constitutes the ultimate ground upon which one may confirm the verses of outward revelation. It validates them experientially, not merely affirms them dogmatically. Thus Maulana Ali states, in the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Bālaghah*, that one purpose of revelation and "the sending forth of prophets" by Allah to mankind was "to unearth for them the buried treasures of the intellects (⟪ar:دفائن العقول⟫)."[^cite-55]

This saying proves central to the spiritual ethos we are considering: the intellect is not stifled by revelation; rather, it is enlivened by it. Revelation is a clarion call to the spiritual depths of the intellect. It cultivates and brings to fruition the seeds of consciousness that Allah has planted within it. The spirit of one's intellect, then, cannot be alien to that which descends as revelation from Allah. On the contrary, it must be fundamentally akin to it. For precisely this reason, one may possess absolute certainty that revelation indeed comes from the Absolute. The question is not one of setting up a dichotomy between intellect *or* revelation, but of seeing the intellect *as* revelation, or at least as one mode of revelation. It (the intellect) is the God-given means by which the individual discerns, affirms, and plumbs the divine "revelations" outside himself—both the signs (*āyāt*) of scripture and those constituted by the signs (*āyāt*) of the natural world. If the human intellect can perceive and recognize revelation, this occurs on account of the fact that both are rooted in the same ultimate source. Prophetic revelation awakens and brings to life the depths of human intellect; it does not replace or displace it. From this perspective, the revealed text is not some extraneous source opposed to intelligence, but rather the objective, outward expression of the very principles that the intellect itself yields to the "inner prophet." By "intellect," then, we mean not simply the faculty of reasoning, but the source of consciousness articulating the human spirit.

The intellect contains unsuspected depths and "buried treasures"—far from being exhausted by its surface operations or by the cognitions and reasonings proper to its purely mental dimension. These considerations return us to the relationship between intellect and reason. Just as there can be no contradiction between *revelation* and *intellect*, so one must avoid setting up a false dichotomy between *reason* and *intellect*. Instead, reason should be perceived as one mode of the intellect. Reason no more contradicts intellect than intellect contradicts revelation. Reason is a mode of the intellect but is not to be equated with it. Similarly, one cannot reduce the universal objectivity of revelation to the subjective particularities of any individual's intellect, or to his or her own "private" revelation. To equate reason with intellect would amount to denying the existence of the "buried treasures" of the intellect—those dimensions transcending rationality and initially "buried" beneath, and thus hidden from, the rational surface. The revelation constituted by the intellect is embedded within human creation, and it is this intellect which most critically defines the human being as such.

Rather than competing intellect against revelation, each must be seen in the light of the other. The substance of revelation cannot be engaged except through the intellect; conversely, the "buried treasures" of the intellect cannot be disclosed except through revelation. This understanding of the intellect brings us near to what Maulana Ali refers to as the "heart" (*qalb*)—that innermost mode of perception capable of "seeing" Allah. It is the heart that can "see" Allah, not through physical vision but through spiritual insight generated by "the realities of faith" (*ḥaqāʾiq al-Īmān*). As Maulana Ali states, in response to one who asked whether he had "seen" his Lord: "Eyes see Him not through sight's observation, but hearts see Him through the true principles and beliefs of faith."[^cite-56]

Returning to the relationship between intellect and reason: between the spiritual depths of the intellect and its rational surface there is both continuity and discontinuity. Continuity manifests in the evident fact that all thinking begins with concepts conveying intelligible meaning. Thought is initially concerned with ideas that are rationally discernible and must bear some relation to the realities they express. Yet discontinuity exists in that reason stops short at ideas "de-fined"—the Arabic word for definition, *ḥadd*, expresses both conceptual definition and limitation, boundary. To transcend the limits imposed by rational definition requires something beyond reason; and this "something more" is what renders the ethos we consider a spiritual one, not a rationalistic one. It is certainly "rational" in that its form is susceptible to reason's scrutiny, but it is not "rationalistic" insofar as its essence is not predicated upon reason alone.

Maulana Ali frequently hints, in highly allusive terms, at the mysteries and marvels—the unearthed treasures of the intellect—which he has directly witnessed through the eye of his purified heart. He alludes to this supra-rational awareness through his bewildering use of apophatic discourse (knowledge of God obtained through negation) and paradoxical utterances, abundant in works attributed to him. In this ethos, the higher the truth, the deeper the paradox. The incommensurability between reality in itself and human conceptions of it is effectively evoked through this creative deployment of paradox. Each human conception of reality requires first "deconstruction" and then "reconstruction." Maulana Ali's paradoxes feed directly into modes of spiritual intuition, contemplation, and realization beyond what reason alone can attain. It is this intuition and contemplation which "re-members" or reconstructs the spiritual realities transcending the categories of reason. The value of paradox, antinomies, apophasis, and negation depends entirely upon the various positive realities which the intellect can plumb, thereby becoming aware of its own hidden depths. The resulting consciousness does not negate reason so much as build upon it, revealing the limits of reason's proper domain. It reveals that reason alone cannot "comprehend" higher realities or express them fully, however indispensable it may be in initiating cognitive movement toward those realities.

When Maulana Ali negates the possibility of human conceptions encompassing divine Reality, what is negated is the limitation—the inevitable corollary of all such conceptions—not the positive content, the *maʿná* (spiritual meaning), of the conception itself. This conception is a necessary starting point for a process of assimilation engaging a deeper awareness than that "defined" by mind, extending far beyond speculative thought. In this light, negation of conceptual limitation should be read as introduction to an absolute affirmation: affirmation of the Absolute, which has no limitation. The spiritual negation of conceptual limitation is upward and "constructive," not nihilistic or deconstructive. Nowhere is this style of discourse more evident than in the famous opening of the first sermon of the *Nahj al-Bālaghah*:

> Praise be to God, whose praise those who speak cannot deliver, whose graces those who count cannot number, whose rightful due those who strive cannot render; He who cannot be grasped by aspirations, nor fathomed by profound intuitions; He whose attribute has no binding limitation, no existing description, no time appointed, no term extended.

The divine attributes (*ṣifāt*) cannot be confined to definition—*laysa li-ṣifātihī ḥaddun maḥdūd*—and yet they are affirmed as real. They are described (*mawṣūf*) by the very words of Quranic revelation, even though they have no "description." Allah is described as the Merciful, the Compassionate, the Almighty; yet Maulana Ali affirms that these very attributes cannot be defined, bounded, or described. The paradox sharpens when he speaks of the perfection of *ikhlaṣ* (sincere devotion)—the "rendering sincere" or purification of one's devotion to Allah. It is to divest (*nafy*) Allah of all attributes. On one hand, attributes are affirmed; on the other, they are negated. The rational mind apprehends Allah only through attributes verbally expressed in the Qur'an. Yet Maulana Ali appears to negate these same attributes, depriving the mind of any convenient conceptual handle by which to grasp divine Reality. This simultaneous affirmation and negation of attributes raises an apparently irresolvable contradiction, yet in doing so, it heightens receptivity to a deeper mode of knowing. This style of discourse is designed to awaken latent awareness inscribed in the heart or depths of the intellect. This awareness invites the seeker to "dive" into the depths of "buried" knowledge within the intellect. The very shock of apparent contradiction precipitates a leap of consciousness from formal reason to supra-rational contemplation of the spirit.

Consider the words Maulana Ali speaks immediately after affirming that his heart has indeed witnessed Allah, not by outward vision but through "the realities of faith": "My Lord is not described in terms of dimension (⟪ar:بُعْد⟫), nor motion (⟪ar:حرکۃ⟫), nor rest (⟪ar:سکون⟫), nor through coming or going. He is subtle in subtlety but not described by subtlety. He is magnificent in magnificence but not described by magnificence. He is great in greatness but not described by greatness."[^cite-57]

Attributes are again affirmed and negated. Allah possesses these attributes, but is not reducible to them. We may begin to think of Allah through these categories, but we cannot reduce Him to our limited conceptions. The attributes surely afford something by which the divine nature can be understood, yet this understanding remains fragmentary, never exhaustive. It therefore requires negation, so that fuller understanding may emerge. The initial conceptual orientation to divine Reality must be both negated and sublimated—negated as mental conception, while its positive content is elevated through spiritual intuition. Only then can the two-dimensional "portrait" defined by reason receive the third dimension of depth. But this third dimension emerges only after reason's insufficiency is clearly understood. Paradox produces this cognitive shock, which reveals the pretension of reason in its effort to disclose the nature of the Absolute.

One observes in Maulana Ali's discourses a repeated combination of affirmation and denial, mirroring the negation and affirmation found in Islam's first testimony: *Lā ilāha* ("No Allah")—the negation (*nafy*)—followed by *Illā Allah* ("except Allah")—the affirmation (*Isbāt*). This illuminates how the perfection of *ikhlaṣ*, as Maulana Ali describes it, is gained through the negation of attributes "in order to" affirm the infinitude of divine Reality. Each attribute is to be "negated," but only insofar as it is conceived by the limited mind. Negation is necessary because at the mental level, each attribute "excludes" not only other attributes but also the object of attribution itself (the divine Essence). Where definitions exist, limits exist, resulting in exclusions. However, the reality of the attribute is unlimited by virtue of its absolute identity with the Essence. The attribute that has been "negated" is paradoxically affirmed as being nothing "but" Allah: *illā Allah*. Thus one may place each negated attribute in place of the negation (*nafy*) of the first testimony, arriving at formulations such as: *Lā Raḥmāna illā-Llāh*, "There is no All-Compassionate except Allah"; *Lā Raḥīma illā-Llāh*, "There is no All-Merciful except Allah."

In this respect, the apparent negation of attributes becomes an affirmation of their true identity, disconnecting any restrictive association with the poverty of mental conceptions. The attributes are not superimposed upon the Essence; rather, they are absolutely one with it. Attributes are differentiable from Essence only in conception, not in existence. The Essence indeed comprises all mentioned attributes—greatness, subtlety, and others—yet is infinitely more than their sum. These attributes are infinite only within and as the Essence. Maulana Ali's explanation of this paradox constitutes, in fact, a commentary upon the Quranic verse:

> ⟪quran 23:91⟫  
> Glorified be Allah above all that they attribute to Him!

The infinitude of Allah requires the negation of all that mind can conceive on the human plane. But it does not negate all that the heart can "see" through spiritual vision. This very negation on the plane of reason calls out to something deeper, understood only in light of spiritual practice—the reflective, devotional, or contemplative context always assumed for those hearing Maulana Ali's sermons and discourses. The heart, the symbolic seat of contemplative intelligence, calls out to be "polished," and this is precisely what "remembrance of Allah" (*Dhikr Allāh*) accomplishes. This is why, according to Maulana Ali, remembrance of Allah is "the most excellent form of worship."[^cite-58]

Yet even at the level of practice, paradox persists. For "the reality of remembrance" is not attained until "you forget yourself" in your remembrance of Allah. It is not merely the rational mind and its concepts that require negation or "deconstruction." Rather, the individual "soul" itself—the locus of all cognition, rational or supra-rational—must be negated or "deconstructed." This final denouement, or annihilation (*fanāʾ*), represents the ultimate paradox.

In Maulana Ali's perspective, the intellect must be seen first and foremost as a spiritual faculty. The spirit must be appreciated not only as the source of the intellect but also as the source of life itself. Without the intellect, the spirit reduces to unconscious existence; without the spirit, the intellect tends toward lifeless thought. This is why the Qur'an, describing man's creation, states that Allah "breathed" His spirit into Adam:

> ⟪quran 32:7‑9⟫  
> Who made everything He has created good, and He began the creation of man from clay. Then He made his offspring from semen of worthless water. Then He fashioned him in due proportion, and **breathed** into him the soul, and He gave you hearing, sight and hearts. Little is the thanks you give!

The following passage from the first sermon illustrates how Maulana Ali comments upon this infusion of divine spirit into man:

> Then Allah (may He be glorified), gathered up a quantity of dust, from some hard earth and some soft, some sweet earth and some salty, and poured water onto it until it was pure, then kneaded into it some moisture until it stuck together. He then fashioned from this, a form with curves and joints, limbs and parts. He compacted it until it held tightly together. He solidified it until it became as sounding clay, assigning to it a measured duration and a known consummation. Then He breathed into it of His spirit, and it stood up as a human being, endowed with intellectual powers with which to reflect, thoughts by which to conduct himself, limbs to put to service, instruments at his disposal, and knowledge with which to discern between the true and the false.

It is the "in-spiration"—literally, the "in-breathing" and insertion of the spirit—of the breath of Allah's spirit that produces consciousness and life. Death, conversely, is "ex-piration," the departure of the spirit. Life, in the absence of intention to be true to one's spirit, is a kind of death. Through this spirit, inanimate matter is brought to life and given awareness of itself. It generates not only knowledge of other forms but also knowledge of self. This intimate intertwining of the gift of life with the capacity to know—existence as conscious, knowledge as existential—forms one of the foundations of any truly "spiritual" perspective. The matter is not one of contrasting spirit with body, but of realizing that the infusion of spirit "into" the body brings the body to life. For this reason, "spiritual" knowledge enlivens the hearts:

> ⟪quran 8:24⟫  
> O you who believe! Answer Allah and His messenger when he calls you to that which will give you life, and know that Allah comes in between a person and his heart. And verily to Him you shall be gathered.

According to Maulana Ali, one of the most important aspects of this "call" from Allah to humanity is to "remind" them of the buried treasures of the intellect.

Existence is the bestowal of the conscious spirit upon the body. Hence the *ʿAql* (intellect)—the principle of articulation for the spirit—is not merely a mental faculty; it is the constitution of our true life. Mental faculty is but one mode of the intellect's operation, and by no means the only one. According to the sayings of Maulana Ali, the *ʿaql* is multifaceted, comprising aspects that in our times are scarcely associated with intelligence.

To be "spiritual," then, is to be true to one's intellect—to the treasures buried deep within it—and not merely to the rational functions operating on its surface. For Maulana Ali, the "true intellectual" (*al-ʿāqil*) is one who not only "thinks" correctly but also "acts" ethically, and at the deepest level seeks to "realize" ultimate Reality. The intellectual is defined by him as "one who puts all things in their proper place." Because this is the very definition of justice, only the true intellectual can be fully "just." Only one possessing the ability to see things as they "truly are" can place them in their right place. Therefore, thought, action, and realization are all concerns of the true intellectual—the *ʿāqil*. Correct thinking, impeccable virtue, and authentic being are inseparably bound together with the intellect in this spiritual ethos. These aspects become clearer in light of Maulana Ali's discourse to Kumayl, to which we now turn.


<!-- section 25 (id=5713, raw_sort=25): The Discourse to Kumayl -->

## The Discourse to Kumayl

This discourse has been the subject of numerous commentaries throughout the centuries. Its paramount significance lies, among other things, in its profoundly esoteric character—a quality that has occasioned scholarly reflection across generations of spiritual interpreters.

The Commander of the Faithful, ʿAlī ibn Abī Ṭālib, mysteriously summons one of his disciples, Kumayl, outside the city of Kufa to a graveyard, there to impart the following private counsel:

> O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, truly these hearts are vessels, and the best of them are those which hold the most. So retain from me that which I say to you. People are divided into three types: a lordly knower (⟪ar:فَعَالِمٌ رَبَّانِيٌّ⟫, *fāʿālim rabbānī*), one who seeks knowledge (⟪ar:متعلم⟫, *mutaʿallim*) for the sake of deliverance, and the common folk following just anyone, swaying with every current, not desiring to be illumined by the light of knowledge, nor seeking refuge from any strong support.
>
> O Kumayl, knowledge is better than wealth, for knowledge guards you, while you must guard wealth; and wealth diminishes as it is spent, while knowledge increases as it is disbursed; and the results of wealth disappear with the disappearance of wealth. O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, the wise apprehension of knowledge is a religion by which Allah is worshipped. Through it, man acquires obedience in his life and a good name after his death. And knowledge is a judge, while wealth is judged.
>
> O Kumayl ibn Ziyad, those who hoard wealth perish even while they live, but the knowers endure for as long as time subsists; their material forms are absent, but their spiritual images in the hearts are present. Ah! what abundant knowledge is here (pointing to his breast); if only I could find one to bear it. Yes, I found one who was quick to understand, but he could not be trusted with it, exploiting the tools of religion for the sake of worldly gain, empowering himself with the graces of Allah against His slaves, and with His proofs against His friends. Then there was one who was obedient to the bearers of truth, but lacking heartfelt insight; at the first appearance of obscurity, doubt was kindled in his heart. So alas, neither this one nor that! Nor one who is greedy for pleasure, submissive to passion, nor one obsessed with acquisition and accumulation—neither of them is a guardian of religion in any respect. They resemble nothing so much as grazing cattle! Thus does knowledge die with the death of its bearers.
>
> But indeed! By Allah, the earth will never be empty of one (Imam) who establishes the proof of Allah whether overtly with publicity or fearfully in obscurity, lest Allah's proofs and elucidations come to naught. But such as these—how many are they and where? By Allah, they may be the smallest in number, but with Allah they are the greatest in rank. Through them Allah preserves His proofs and elucidations, so that they entrust them to their compeers and sow them in the hearts of those resembling them. Through them, knowledge penetrates the reality of insight. They rejoice in their intimacy with the spirit of certainty; they make easy what the extravagant find harsh; they befriend that by which the ignorant are estranged. With their bodies they keep company with the world, while their spirits are tied to the transcendent realm. They are the vicegerents of Allah on His earth, summoners to His religion. Ah! how I long to see them! Go now, Kumayl, as you will.

For our purposes here, it suffices to reflect upon four pivotal sentences of this khutba (sermon), wherein we are given the description of those who bear the title of "lordly knower" (fāʿālim rabbānī)—those who are fewest in number but greatest in the sight of Allah, and through whom "His religion" is sustained. By "His religion," we mean the divine reality of religion itself, the pure essence of the faith, which is perfect and immutable, in contrast to "religion" in the ordinary sense, whose formal institutions are subject to human vicissitudes.[^cite-59] It is by means of the knowledge of the true knowers that religion remains true to itself, and it is only through such sages that religion truly "lives" in the world.

### Through them, knowledge penetrates the reality of insight

Knowledge can remain merely abstract, far removed from the "reality of insight" (ḥaqīqat al-baṣīra). Yet knowledge contains a latent potential that calls out to be realized. It must be enlivened in order to "penetrate" into something deeper than the words and concepts that articulate its own form—something beyond the immediately intelligible meaning on the surface. Degrees of knowledge are clearly implied here, as is the decisive avoidance of all literalism and superficiality, the rejection of facile equations between factual information and actual knowledge, let alone wisdom.[^cite-60] The shift of consciousness required for the "penetration" of knowledge is indicated graphically by ʿAlī's gesture of pointing to his breast.

The word "penetrate" renders the Arabic *hajama*, whose primary meanings are "to assault," "to take by storm," "to charge"—meanings that illuminate a crucial aspect of the teaching. There exists within conceptual knowledge a latent force, one might almost say an "explosive" power, deriving from the reality (*haqīqa*, the spiritual essence) of knowledge itself. It is this inner force that calls out to be made real, through *tahaqquq* (investigation)—realization in depth, not mere factual verification on the surface.[^cite-61] This *haqīqa* can be indicated, but not captured, by the words and concepts that constitute its formal communicable aspect. By means of this inner force, which the outer conceptual form both hides and evokes, knowledge is capable of effecting a shift of consciousness from one degree of knowledge to another.

An indirect, mental conception of reality thus paves the way for a "penetrating," existential perception of reality. The "reality of insight" opens the intelligence to reality as such, whereas ordinary "insight," like abstract knowledge, remains only at the superficial level of understanding. The same idea appears in a celebrated saying of ʿAlī relating to true understanding, which comes to plumb and thus truly "know" the deeper content of knowledge, its own buried dimensions:

> He who understands, knows the depth of knowledge. (⟪ar:من فھم علم گوہر العلم⟫)

The effort required to attain true knowledge, as opposed to the mere accumulation of facts or details, is indicated likewise in this aphorism:

> Through learning and assimilating knowledge—such that it becomes deeply rooted in one's consciousness—knowledge is attained. (⟪ar:بالتَّعَلُّم یَنَالَ العِلمْ⟫)

The urgency of the need to realize the immanent power within knowledge—to move from abstract to concrete, from potential to actual, from static to dynamic, from formal to essential—is effectively evoked by the use of the word *hajama*, which connotes a military assault. This terminology alerts one to the perils of confusing the initial understanding of an idea with its full comprehension, and intellectual comprehension with the dynamic interiorization of spirituality itself. This interiorization is a process of infinite unfolding within, not the simple registering of some external fact. It thus requires the effective integration of knowledge within being; what is encountered as knowledge from without must be transformed into being from within, woven into the deepest texture of one's existence. Knowledge therefore demands not merely the attention of the mind but the total orientation of the soul.

In this ethos, then, the effective integration of knowledge (*tawhīd*, the unification principle) within being is unattainable without the participation of the whole soul. This, in turn, requires the elimination of all vices that flow from egocentricity. Thus, the *ʿāqil* (the true intellectual) is described by ʿAlī as being constantly engaged in "struggle against his own soul"—against the lower inclinations, the passionate whims, and the distracting caprices of the ego.[^cite-62] All these vices, far from being confined to the plane of morality, encroach upon the domain of knowledge. This becomes fully apparent when knowledge is grasped as a concrete vision of things, not merely as notional or conceptual understanding. The vices are then regarded as so many clouds obscuring the vision proper to the "reality of insight." According to the Qurʾān, evil actions become "rust" that covers the heart:

> Nay! But on their hearts is the *Ran* (rust) which they used to earn. (⟪quran 83:14⟫)

The *ʿāqil* (Intellectual) is thus engaged in constant inner struggle against the tendencies which not only sully the soul—they also blind the heart. As a virtuous person, the *ʿāqil* opposes vice on the moral plane, because it contradicts virtue. But vice is also opposed for another reason, situated on the higher plane of spiritual reality. It contradicts the nature of the Real; it obscures the reality of insight, blinding the "eye" of the heart which alone can "see" Allah.

It is not a question here of denying the validity of the moral perspective; rather, the stress falls on the deeper motivation propelling the *ʿāqil* toward virtue. This motivation specifically transcends the moral plane while comprising it; morality emerges here as fruit or effect, and spirituality as seed or cause. By drawing attention to the intellectual aspect of the *ʿāqil*'s motivation for being rid of vices, ʿAlī brings out more clearly how immorality is rooted in ignorance. The uprooting of this ignorance, for the true intellectual, is thus tantamount to dissolving the very substance of the vices themselves. This uprooting requires great effort—a relentless battle within, which is indeed the most formidable of all battles, *al-jihād al-akbar* (the greatest struggle), as the Prophet defined it.

One should take careful note of the following sayings of ʿAlī on the importance of this inner struggle:

> Struggling against the soul through knowledge—such is the mark of the intellect.
> 
> The strongest people are those who are strongest against their own souls.
> 
> Truly, one who fights his own soul in obedience to Allah and does not sin against Him has the rank of the righteous martyr in Allah's eyes.
> 
> The ultimate battle is that of a man against his own soul.
> 
> He who knows his soul fights it.
> 
> No jihad is more excellent than the jihad of the soul.

In another saying, ʿAlī clarifies what the Prophet meant by defining the greatest jihad as that of the soul (*al-nafs*). It is not so much a question of fighting against the soul, but the battle **for** the soul itself. This emerges from the metaphor given by the Imam to define the struggle: *al-ʿAql* (the Intellect) is the leader of the forces of *al-Raḥmān* (the Compassionate); *al-hawā* (caprice, whim, desire) commands the forces of *al-Shaytān* (the Satan). The soul (*al-nafs*) vacillates between them, susceptible to the attraction of both (⟪ar:مُتَجَاذِبَہ بینھما⟫), and enters into the "domain" of whichever of the two will triumph.

These sayings underscore the fact that, for the true intellectual, the soul in its entirety must be "conquered" and enlisted on the side of the intellect. It is not a question of destroying the lower soul but of ridding it of the destructive influence of its caprice, its egoistic whims, subjectivism, and individualism—all of these notions being connoted by the word *hawā*. The Prophet, we are told in the Qurʾān, speaks not from *hawā*; rather:

> Nor does he speak of desire. It is only an Inspiration that is inspired. (⟪quran 53:3-4⟫)

The divine objectivity of revelation is here contrasted with the human subjectivity of passional whim. In the context of the holy war within, an analogous polarity is brought into focus by ʿAlī's sayings. The objectivity of the intellect must prevail against the subjectivity of individual caprice and egotistic desire.

It is the intellect that brings to the forefront the divine power of *al-Raḥmān* and which combats the forces of *al-Shaytān*, represented by *al-hawā*.

The word *hawā* is further employed in the Qurʾān in the sense of the desires stemming from the ego, by means of which the ego becomes a kind of false god:

> Have you seen him who has taken as his deity his own desire? Would you then be a *witness* over him? (⟪quran 25:43⟫)

This is the subtle or hidden idolatry (⟪ar:الشرک الخفی⟫, *al-shirk al-khafī*) to which the Prophet referred as being more hidden than a black ant crawling on a black stone in a moonless night. To eliminate this idolatry of the ego, some force higher than that of the ego itself must be engaged, for it is impossible for the ego to be controlled or dominated by something pertaining to the ego itself. This higher force is the divine Reality, access to which is made possible through the power of the intellect and the graces of faith. This complementarity between the effort to overcome the egoism of *hawā* and the influx of divine grace is well expressed by the following verse of the Qurʾān:

> But as for him who feared standing before his Lord, and restrained himself from impure evil desires, and lusts, verily, Paradise will be his abode. (⟪quran 79:40-41⟫)

The soul's fundamental energy is not to be destroyed but converted and redirected away from the transient objects of individualistic desire, away from the impulses of *al-Shaytān*, toward the One, true object that is expressed by *al-Raḥmān*. This "conquest" of the soul involves much more than attaining to some mental conception. If one is to be rendered capable of perceiving that truth in all its dimensions and ramifications on the different planes of being, then it is not only the mind, the will, and the character that must be integrated into the truth perceived by the intellect, but the fundamental disposition of the soul itself.

It is important to stress that, for ʿAlī, the need to win the soul over in its totality follows naturally from his understanding of *Tawḥīd* (*tawhīd*, the affirmation and realization of divine oneness), the most fundamental principle of Islam. It is crucial not to lose sight of the fact that *Tawḥīd* is both noun and verb. It is often simply translated as "oneness" or "unity," but it literally means "affirming one" (the affirmation of, and belief in, the oneness of Allah, which is the outward, theological meaning) or "realizing the one" (the realization that there is **but** one reality, which is the inner, spiritual meaning). Thus, in ʿAlī's perspective, the absolutely Real is perceived not only as being one but also as making one, or integrating! The Real is not only unique; it is also unifying. *Tawḥīd* then comes to mean not merely affirming the oneness of Allah but also "being" one in order to reflect perfectly the One; to know Allah is to be "integrated."[^cite-63] One observes here a link with the etymology of the word *ʿaqal*, whose primary meaning is "to bind" or "to restrain." In the later mystical tradition, the limitative aspect of the word is emphasized—the intellect being that which defines and encloses meaning, as opposed to the heart which intuits and discloses spiritual truth. Here, by contrast, one sees that the intellect is that which "binds together" the disparate elements of the soul into an organic unity. This principle is expressed in the first of the following series of ʿAlī's sayings, each of which merits deep reflection:

> He who knows Allah integrates himself. (⟪ar-quote:مَنْ عَرَفَ اللہ تَوَحَّدُ⟫)
> 
> He who knows his soul disengages himself.
> 
> He who knows people isolates himself.
> 
> He who knows the world withholds himself.

Elsewhere this total adherence to the Real is referred to by ʿAlī as "sincerity," *sidq* or *ikhlāṣ* (*ikhlas*, purity of intention), terms which denote far more than simply truthfulness. The root of the word *ikhlāṣ* is significant: the word *khalaṣa* means to be utterly pure, true to one's essence, unadulterated by any extraneous substance. This aspect of the meaning is well expressed in the following saying of ʿAlī:

> Make purely for Allah your action and your knowledge, your love and your hatred, your taking and your leaving, your speech and your silence. (⟪ar-quote:أخلص لی اللہ العلم و العمل والحب و العداوۃ، والقبض و المغادرۃ، والنطق و الصمت⟫)

To be totally sincere, then, means to make oneself "purely" for Allah, to have no "stain" of impurity—here understood as what is other than Allah—present in one's devotion, action, intention, and even sentiment. One must love and hate only in function of one's relationship with Allah. Of all the tendencies which insinuate themselves within one's motivations and intentions, the most pernicious is egotism. *Ikhlāṣ* (pure sincerity) involves the active effort to BE, THINK, and ACT purely for Allah—that is, with selflessness. Hence the stress placed by ʿAlī on the holy war within the soul as the "ultimate battle." One who succeeds in this battle attains the rank of the "righteous martyr." The victor here is the martyr because he has sacrificed his lower life for the sake of Allah, with whom is the real life, that which is witnessed (*shahīd*, both martyr and witness in the sense of the original Greek meaning of martyres) by the intellect, the only element within human consciousness capable of objectively looking upon the soul as "the other."

To reiterate: "Struggling against the soul through knowledge—such is the mark of the intellect." The intellectual in this battle also bears "witness" (*al-shahīd*). He bears witness to Allah through the intellect's sacrifice of that which opposes the truth of Allah; and the key opponent in question is his own egotism, his own "false life." To have perfect *ikhlāṣ*, then, is to be rid of all hypocritical self-preoccupation and to install in its place a sincere theocentricity, a mode of being "centered" upon Allah, not oneself. Thus, *ikhlāṣ* is to be for Allah, to belong to Allah, in the sense indicated by the Qurʾānic verse:

> Say: "My prayers, my asceticism, my life and my death are for Allah, the Lord of the worlds." (⟪quran 6:162⟫)

The fact that worship is the first thing mentioned in this verse is of particular significance for anyone wishing to appreciate the nature of ʿAlī's spiritual ethos (⟪ar:مزاج⟫, *mizāj*). What must be stressed in presenting this ethos is the indispensable role performed by worship and devotion. One of the key distinguishing features of ʿAlī's spiritual ethos is the stress he places on prayer, taking this term in its widest sense to include all those means—from formal worship and supplication to meditation, reflection, remembrance, and contemplation—by which the individual is oriented and attuned to the Real, *al-Ḥaqq*.

Worship is, therefore, an integral aspect of *ikhlāṣ*, in the very measure that worship relates to and sustains a fundamental dimension of the human soul. That within the soul which yearns to worship and is satisfied only by the sustenance given through worship must be given its due. In a spiritual context, if worship is marginalized, one's sincerity is compromised, for one is not being sincere to Allah, not being "just" to Allah—not rendering Allah His due. And the spiritual logic of ʿAlī dictates that if one is not just with Allah, one cannot be just to His creatures. He says: "Whoever establishes well-being between himself and Allah, Allah establishes well-being between him and mankind."

One might also quote here the verse of the Qurʾān:

> And worship your Lord until certainty comes to you. (⟪quran 15:99⟫)

The quest for knowledge, then, demands total dedication if it is to yield up its inner riches, and this dedication includes that transformation of consciousness shaped by total concentration on the divine Reality—prayer in one of its deepest modes. In this form of prayer, the intensity of "dedication" is such that there is no room in one's consciousness for anything other than the object to which one is dedicated—the "remembrance" (⟪ar:ذکر⟫, *dhikr*) of Allah, which strictly implies the forgetting of everything else, including, preeminently, oneself. As mentioned earlier, the spiritual reality of *dhikr* can only be attained when "you forget your soul in your remembrance." Concentration upon the divine Reality as the supreme object of knowledge, rendered present through the Name or Names of Allah, is thus a mode of prayer. And for it to be effective, it requires both presence of mind and a serious engagement with the will. It is far from sufficient to pray absent-mindedly in a mechanical fashion. In this connection ʿAlī says: "He who prays without exertion is like one who shoots arrows without a bow." Likewise, there is certainly nothing passive or easy about the process of acquiring knowledge: "Knowledge calls out for action; if it is answered, it is of avail; otherwise it departs."

The same idea is expressed by ʿAlī more forcefully in this saying: "When you hear knowledge being imparted, cleave to it; do not mix it up with jest, lest hearts spew it forth." Knowledge that is not taken seriously will be of no avail. It remains "undigested" and refuses to be assimilated by the "heart," the faculty that has access to the "reality of insight" and which can thus "see" Allah. In fact, it is not the individual who "assimilates" knowledge to himself, but knowledge—or the **reality of being expressed by true knowledge**—that assimilates the individual: "Acquire knowledge, and true life will acquire you." The relationship between life and consciousness, so central to the intrinsic function of the spirit as discussed earlier, is again stressed in this maxim. At first, knowledge is sought by the individual and consciousness is cultivated; then, life "seeks" the individual as his being makes itself "real" or realizes itself more fully within him. In other words, the individual first makes an active effort to "integrate" knowledge within his being; but at a certain point, this activity is transformed into passive receptivity—being, "true life," integrates the individual within itself. To return to ʿAlī's guidance to Kumayl: "Through them, knowledge penetrates the reality of insight." It is not the knowers who penetrate; it is knowledge that penetrates into the reality of insight, doing so through them, and this in the very measure of their effacement within knowledge—an effacement which is the shadow cast by their effective integration within the life that "acquires" them.

### They rejoice in their intimacy with the spirit of certainty

The next sentence in the discourse to Kumayl is of particular importance in helping us appreciate the sheer happiness that flows from the "life of the spirit." In the Arabic expression: "⟪ar:وبشِّرُ روح الیقین⟫" (*wa-bāshirū rūḥ al-yaqīn*), the primary meaning of the word *bāshara* is to have intimate experience of a thing, to come into direct contact with it. This form of the verb also relates to joy. Hence, "rejoice in intimate contact" better conveys something of the meaning of this single word. To touch the "spirit of certainty" (*rūḥ al-yaqīn*) is to come into contact, not with some cold logical fact, the conclusion of a purely mental process, but rather to engage deeply with the object of certainty, with that of which one is certain—ultimately, nothing less than the divine itself. Since this reality is infinitely more beatific than any conceivable paradisal bliss—according to the Qurʾān, the divine *Riḍwān* (beatitude, contentment) is "greater" than Paradise:

> Allah has promised to the believers—men and women—Gardens under which rivers flow to dwell therein forever, and beautiful mansions in Gardens of ʿAdn. But the greatest bliss is the Good Pleasure of Allah. That is the supreme success. (⟪quran 9:72⟫)

It cannot but bestow joy upon the soul which effectively opens itself to that reality. In the words of ʿAlī: "Learning and reflecting upon knowledge is the delight of the knowers." This is what one might call an "epistemology of joy," for "the most joyous of mankind is the intellectual (*al-ʿāqil*)." This joyous epistemology—which flies in the face of modern scientific conceptions of knowledge as dry, cold, and abstract—should be considered carefully, as it reveals a crucial dimension of the spiritual knowledge proper to the Imam's perspective and indeed to all spiritual traditions worthy of the name. Coming into contact with truths should impart happiness, and this happiness obtains in the measure that the truths in question are fundamental to one's being or to Being as such. The two modes of being are ultimately identical, given the uniqueness, or the "one-and-onliness," of Being. That which has no second, no other, cannot enter the category of number; being is indivisible. Hence, the being of the individual cannot ultimately be different from Being as such, however great the existential disparity—or rather, incommensurability—between the individual and Allah. True knowledge is knowledge of Being; thus it is of necessity a kind of self-discovery, even if knowledge of the Absolute is impossible without absolute "self" abandonment. The highest discovery calls for the deepest effacement.

However, in the initial stages of this process, true knowledge, acquired by the intellectual, is a kind of self-discovery that imparts the happiness of a homecoming, a return from the exile of ignorance. This follows because "the most intellectual of people" is "the one who is closest to Allah" and "the one who is most alive." To be close to Allah is to be fully alive, and this true life gives profound joy—the sheer joy of living. This is a joy proper to existence as such, independent of particular experiences that may or may not be pleasurable.

Here again we see the crucial relationship between life and consciousness: between the fruits of the "inspiration of the spirit of Allah" on one hand, and between this life of spiritual consciousness and joy on the other. Those who are true to the spirit of Allah within them truly possess joy. It is also by reason of the intimate and unshakeable certainty of the divine goodness and compassion that the true *faqīh* (not so much "the jurist" as "one who truly understands") is defined by ʿAlī as "he who does not make people despair of the mercy of Allah, and does not make them lose hope in the gracious spirit of Allah (*rūḥ Allāh*)." Such is the sage's state of immersion in the joyous certainty of Allah's mercy that there is no place for despair. It is a sign of disbelief to despair of this mercy:

> And who despairs of the mercy of his Lord except those who are astray? (⟪quran 15:56⟫)

And:

> When those who believe in Our *Āyāt* come to you, say: *"Salamun ʿAlaikum"*; your Lord has written Mercy for Himself, so that, if any of you does evil in ignorance, and thereafter repents and does righteous good deeds, then surely, He is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful. (⟪quran 6:54⟫)

"My mercy encompasses all things" and "Your Lord has prescribed for Himself mercy" are brought to life fully in the souls that have profound—not merely notional—knowledge of the beatific nature of the divine Reality. They are the ones who truly "understand." They are the true *fuqahāʾ* (those who understand).

This feeling of joy, through intimate knowledge of the mercy of Allah, is inseparable from beauty. And the perception of this beauty is another natural association of the intellect in ʿAlī's perspective. The appreciation of beauty is not merely a question of subjective aesthetics. It is an objective aspect of the function of the intellect. Beauty also takes one to the heart of ethics. The Arabic *Iḥsān* (excellence, virtue) is often simply translated as "virtue" or "excellence." But one of its key literal meanings is "making beautiful"—*Ḥusn* means "beauty." Virtue, *Iḥsān*, can thus be understood as that which "makes the soul beautiful." In this light, the relationship between the intellect, ethics, and aesthetics is more clearly discerned: "The excellence of the intellect," says ʿAlī, "is in the beauty of things outward and inward" (⟪ar:جمال الظواہر والبواطن⟫, *jamāl al-ẓawāhir wa-l-bawātin*). The outward beauty of forms and actions must be complemented by inward beauty—that is, beauty of soul, or virtue in the most integral and penetrating sense of the word. All of this follows naturally from two sayings attributed to the Prophet: "Truly, Allah is beautiful and He loves beauty" and "Assume the character traits of Allah."

Because Allah "loves beauty," this cultivation of "beautiful traits" within oneself makes one lovable to Allah. This process of making oneself lovable to Allah is inseparable from the emulation of the virtues brought to perfection in the soul of the Prophet:

> Say: "If you love Allah, then follow me; Allah will love you and forgive you your sins. And Allah is Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful." (⟪quran 3:31⟫)

This attraction of Allah's love through making oneself beautiful in the fullest sense is alluded to in another saying of ʿAlī: "The foundation of the intellect is the assimilation of love" (⟪ar:أَوَّلُ العَقل التَّوَدُّدْ⟫, *awwal al-ʿaql al-tawaddud*). Rather than simply referring to love as such (⟪ar:وُد/مَوَدَّۃ⟫), ʿAlī draws attention to the need to make oneself lovable to Allah by using the form *tawaddud*. This alludes to the process of integrating or realizing the principle of love within oneself, as well as expressing that love felt inside, outwardly. Love is thus appreciated both as outward radiance and as inner attraction.

To "follow" the Prophet and thus make oneself receptive to the divine influx of love means, among other things, cultivating the "prophetic" virtues—virtues that are prophetic not in the sense that they belong exclusively to the Prophet, but because he is deemed to have realized virtue in all its plenitude, serving as an example for his followers. It is thus unsurprising to find that it is not only beauty and love that articulate the receptivity of the intellect to the divine Reality, but also such qualities as kindness and contentment, courtesy, generosity, and modesty, wise forbearance. None of these moral qualities are absent from the soul of the true intellectual. There can be no wisdom or intellect in the fullest sense if the soul lacks these fundamental qualities. It is thanks to the presence of these virtues that the intellect is able to develop those dimensions of depth and breadth without which it remains confined to the flat, horizontal plane of its own operations. Without virtue it remains confined to the activity of reason alone, which—until touched by faith and deepened by grace—remains unaware of what lies beneath the surface of its own field of activity. It remains oblivious of the "hidden treasures" of the intellect. It remains blind to the need to "integrate" the whole of the soul within the mould of that "integral," or "unifying," virtue which bestows integrity upon the intelligence. Such integration, or "making one," is absolutely fundamental to *tawḥīd*. So *tawḥīd* can now be understood as a dynamic process of **spiritual unification** that is demanded by the idea of the oneness of Allah. It is clearly necessary that truth must inspire the soul. But it is equally necessary that, conversely, virtue "aerate" the intelligence, provide it with the "leaven" (*khamīr*) which allows it to expand to its full dimensions.

Sinful, immoral qualities are grasped in this perspective not only as vices but also as so many blind spots or intellectual dysfunctions. The heart is "diseased" by vice; its "eye" is blinded by them and thus unable to "see" the Real. As is said in the Qurʾān:

> In their hearts is a disease, and Allah has increased their disease. A painful torment is theirs because they used to tell lies. (⟪quran 2:10⟫)

And:

> Verily, it is not the eyes that grow blind, but it is the hearts which are in the breasts that grow blind. (⟪quran 22:46⟫)

As noted above, the vices are all comprised within egotism, and this, in turn, is often described simply as "caprice" (⟪ar:ھوی⟫, *hawā*). In one saying, ʿAlī refers to this conquest over caprice as being tantamount to salvation: "He attains deliverance whose intellect dominates his caprice." Deliverance—*falāḥ*, which comprises both the sense of salvation and beatitude (immense blessedness)—is thus to some degree accessible here and now. It is not just a grace granted after one's demise. This deliverance involves tasting the joy which emanates from certainty, from intimate contact with the spirit of this certainty. As to the means of cultivating receptivity to this spirit of certainty, what is required, over and above obedience to the rules and regulations of the revealed Law, is that combination of purity of intention and sincerity of devotion which is expressed by the word *Ikhlāṣ*. Without this sincerity or purity, complying with those formal rules is nothing but hypocrisy (*Nifāq*) and pretension (*Riyāʾ*), which in turn is defined by ʿAlī as the gravest of all sins, shirk, or polytheism—the attribution of "partners" to Allah: "Know that the slightest pretension (*riyāʾ*) is polytheism." This sentence can be taken as a comment upon one of the most important condemnations of hypocrisy found in the Qurʾān in the verses that refer to those who deny the essence of the faith, even while outwardly conforming to it:

> Have you seen him who denies the Recompense? That is he who repulses the orphan, and urges not the feeding of *al-Miskīn*. So woe unto those performers of *Ṣalāt* (prayers), who delay their *Ṣalāt* from their stated fixed times, those who do good deeds only to be seen, and refuse *al-Māʿūn*. (⟪quran 107:1-7⟫)

This short sūra makes it clear that to be "religious" in one's formal acts of devotion has no value whatsoever unless these acts be integrated into a life of virtue and unless one is performing one's devotion with the right intention—that is, with *ikhlāṣ*. It also helps us see the reason for not referring to ʿAlī's ethos as simply "religious" but as "spiritual," taking the ideals of religion to their most profound application. Without this dimension of depth, religion is meaningless.

"There is no religion for one who has no intellect," says ʿAlī. Likewise, "Action without knowledge is error." The intention motivating the action is what counts. "For action—all action—is dust, except what is purified (accomplished sincerely) within it." This detachment from action as such and the orientation instead toward an inner state of being, from which good actions cannot but emerge, clearly reflects what is found in the Qurʾān:

> It is neither their meat nor their blood that reaches Allah, but it is piety from you that reaches Him. Thus have We made them subject to you that you may magnify Allah for His Guidance to you. And give glad tidings to the one who favors good. (⟪quran 22:37⟫)

### There is no religion for one who has no intellect

One might argue that no group better exemplified the principle expressed in this saying than the *Khawārij* (*Kharijites*). Among this group that rebelled against ʿAlī were numerous "readers" (*qurrāʾ*, sing. *qāriʾ*) of the Qurʾān—people who possessed considerable religious prestige on account of their knowledge of the Qurʾān and their intense worship. Their piety, however, was more apparent than real. It was in fact a "zeal of bitterness," an imitation of true piety. When his supporters made mention of the long night vigils performed by members of this group, ʿAlī gave this sharp reply: "Sleeping with certainty is better than praying with doubt." Certainty, as we have seen, imparts peace and joy. Thus sleep, for one immersed in what ʿAlī calls "the spirit of certainty," is a bodily repose which further deepens the happiness that flows from a good conscience and spiritual certitude. This spiritual certitude is understood as a kind of permanent "wakefulness," in the sense of being awake to the higher realities. Thus bodily sleep does not contradict spiritual wakefulness. As the hadith of the Prophet states, "My eyes sleep, but my heart sleeps not."

In relation to formal worship, the true intellectual is one who is obedient to the divine injunction to pray. But rather than praying in mechanical fashion and believing that the action alone suffices, the *ʿāqil* (Intellectual) knows that such obedience is a necessary but not sufficient condition for achieving receptivity to grace. There must be consciousness of what one is doing and why one is doing it. And it is this awareness, together with virtue or "inward beauty," that attracts the divine acceptance. "The most worthy knowledge for you," says ʿAlī, "is that by virtue of whose presence, action is accepted by Allah."

### They make easy what the extravagant find harsh; they befriend that by which the ignorant are estranged

This sentence takes us, among other things, to the impact made on the soul of the true sages by recollection of the inevitability of death in this world and the consequent necessity of renunciation within it. In the first place, that which is found to be "harsh" by those who are extravagant—the avid, worldly types—is outward adversity, difficulty, and deprivation. These are all circumstances which, in the Qurʾān, are viewed as the trials which inevitably accompany life in this world:

> And certainly, We shall test you with something of fear, hunger, loss of wealth, lives and fruits, but give glad tidings to the patient ones. Who, when afflicted with calamity, say: "Truly! To Allah we belong and truly, to Him we shall return." They are those on whom are the *Ṣalawāt* from their Lord, and receive His Mercy, and it is they who are the guided-ones. (⟪quran 2:155-157⟫)

The wise, the rightly-guided (*al-muhtadūn*), perceive the "blessings" that are showered down upon those who are patient in the face of afflictions—a patience that is the fruit of the completeness of their knowledge of the truth.

> By *Time!* Verily! Man is in loss, except those who believe and do righteous good deeds, and recommend one another to the truth. (⟪quran 103:1-3⟫)

These are the believers who remain steadfast in the face of trials, being firmly rooted in their awareness of their origin and end, and in the fact that "unto Him we are ever-returning." For this reason, they can "make easy" the trials and difficulties of life, whereas the "extravagant" experience them solely in their aspect of harshness and bitterness. The "ease" that permanently exists in all difficulty is hidden from the ignorant but discerned by the wise.

> So verily, WITH hardship, there is relief, verily, WITH hardship, there is relief. (⟪quran 94:5-6⟫)

The wise go even further than just anticipating "ease" as the inevitable fruit of hardship. They taste this fruit already as the seed hidden within hardship itself, for they actually "befriend that by which the ignorant are estranged."

We can understand one level of meaning here with the help of this saying of ʿAlī: "Death is more intimate to me than the breast of the mother is to the suckling baby." For in the verse cited above, the mention of trials and difficulties is preceded by a reminder that those who are slain "in the path of Allah" are not really dead: "No! they are alive but you do not perceive." We have here not simply an affirmation of the life hereafter, but also an allusion to the esoteric meaning of being "slain" for Allah—⟪ar:الفنا فی اللہ⟫ (*al-fanāʾ fī Allāh*, extinction in Allah). This intimacy with death, mentioned by ʿAlī, relates to this "death" while still alive, which he also alludes to in another saying: "I am the death of the dead." It is because life in the world itself is a kind of death—a limitation. This life is privation (a state in which things essential for human well-being are lacking), in contrast to the infinite abundance of the divine realms. Therefore, death in this world is tantamount to true life. As the Qurʾān says:

> And this life of the world is only amusement and play! Verily, the home of the Hereafter, that is the life indeed, if they but knew. (⟪quran 29:64⟫)

However, it is important to stress that it is not the physical world as such that is to be negated; rather, it is worldliness—the egotistic attachment to this world—that must be overcome. Here we need to dwell a little on one of the key principles of ʿAlī's perspective, stressed repeatedly throughout the *Nahj al-Balāgha*. This is the principle of *zuhd* (asceticism, detachment, renunciation), a word which cannot be translated by any single English term. It comprises several meanings: asceticism, austerity, abstention, detachment, doing without, making little of. The following passage from the *Nahj al-Balāgha* expresses well the attitude of the *zāhid* (the ascetic) toward this world:

> The world is an abode for which annihilation is ordained, and for its people departure from it is decreed. It is sweet and green, and it hastens to one who seeks it. It confounds the heart that gazes upon it. So set out from it with the best of the provision available to you, and ask not from it above what suffices and seek not from it more than what can be attained.

What is this "best of provision" (*zād*, provisions for a journey)? In many places in the *Nahj*, it is described as *taqwā* (piety, fear of Allah, consciousness of Allah), another word which is difficult to translate. "I strongly encourage you, O slaves of Allah, to *taqwā*, which is the true provision," says ʿAlī. This provision for the next world is only attained in the measure of one's detachment from the perishable aspects of the "life of this world." The Qurʾān states:

> Whoever wishes for the quick-passing, We readily grant him what We will for whom We like. Then, afterwards, We have appointed for him Hell, he will burn therein disgraced and rejected. (⟪quran 17:18⟫)

The despicable aspect of this world is described in vivid terms by ʿAlī:

- By Allah, this world of yours is more contemptible in my eyes than a chewed-up bone of a pig in the hand of a leper.
- The image of this world is that of a snake—soft to touch but filled with virulent poison.
- This world is "as worthless to me as that which drips from the nose of a goat."

It is the grandeur and beauty of Allah that render this world insignificant and distasteful. It is only in the light of the Absolute that the pleasures and attractions of the world appear fleeting and illusory: "The greatness of the Creator for you belittles creatures in your view." In similar vein, ʿAlī refers to a "brother in Allah": "What made him great in my eyes was the smallness of the world in his [eyes]."

This, according to Qurʾānic usage of *zuhd*, is the real meaning of *zuhd*. In Sūrat Yūsuf, the brothers of Yūsuf "deemed him of little value" in selling him for a small price:

> And they sold him for a low price—for a few Dirhams. And they were of those who regarded him insignificant. (⟪quran 12:20⟫)

They were described as being, in relation to him, "*zāhidīn*" (those who regard as insignificant). The world, in ʿAlī's perspective, is likewise deemed of little value. Not that it is altogether valueless, but that, in relation to the Hereafter, in relation to eternity, in relation to ultimate reality, it is rendered all but insignificant. All created things are reduced to infinitesimal, fleeting shadows, destined for annihilation:

> Everything will perish except His Face. His is the Decree, and to Him you shall be returned. (⟪quran 28:88⟫)

However, the world is not deprived of all value. Rather:

> The seven heavens and the earth and all that is therein glorify Him, and there is not a thing but glorifies His Praise. But you understand not their glorification. Truly, He is Ever Forbearing, Oft-Forgiving. (⟪quran 17:44⟫)

Everything in the world, all the creatures in existence, thus proclaim the infinite creativity of the Creator. To help us imaginatively comprehend the mystery of this universal praise, the Qurʾān tells us:

> See you not that Allah, He it is Whom glorify whosoever is in the heavens and the earth, and the birds with wings out-spread. Of each one He knows indeed his *Ṣalāt* (prayer) and his glorification, and Allah is All-Aware of what they do. (⟪quran 24:41⟫)

It is thus not at all contradictory for ʿAlī to both despise the world and yet express in his description the beauties of virgin nature—including that unsurpassable marvel, the peacock. This comes in a sermon which includes descriptions of birds in general, mentioning also ants, serpents, and elephants.

It is noteworthy that the sermon concludes with an exiting description of Paradise, comparing the marvels of this world with the delights of the next. When compared to the latter, all terrestrial phenomena pale into insignificance. For once the "eye of the heart" is directed to Paradise, "your soul would become averse to the marvels of this world—its passions, its pleasures and its embellished scenery." It might seem contradictory to bring together such apparently opposed qualities as joy in certainty (discussed above) and the "befriending" of hardship. But the latter is in fact the fruit of the former. All difficulties without exception are endured, thanks to the spiritual resources made available by faith, combined with intellectual assimilation. Rather, in the measure that faith is intellectually assimilated and thus becomes *maʿrifah* (gnosis), this gnosis comprises a certainty so deep and so complete that "even if the veil be lifted," it cannot increase, according to the famous saying of ʿAlī: "Were the veil (of this world) to be removed, I would not increase in certitude" (⟪ar:لو کشف الغطاء ما ازدت یقینا⟫, *law kushif al-ghitāʾ mā azdat yaqīnan*).[^cite-64]

It is possible to believe, at one level of the mind, that "there is no deity but Allah." ʿAlī, however, draws attention to what this apparently simple belief should imply when it is "sincere" or "pure":

> I bear witness that there is no deity but Allah alone who has no partner—a testimony whose sincerity has been tested and whose essence has been assimilated with conviction. (⟪ar:مُعتَقَداً مُصَعصَھَا⟫, *muʿtaqadan, muṣaʿṣaʿahā*)

The word *muṣaʿṣaʿ* refers to the choicest and purest part of any substance—that which constitutes its essence. The root of the word *maṣṣa* means "to suck." The idea here is that the liquid or juice of a substance or fruit contains its essence, that which is "sucked" out of it. In the present context, ʿAlī is stressing the spiritual substance of his testimony, as opposed to mere verbal affirmation or mental comprehension of the words. He affirms a heartfelt connection with the One for which his testimony bears witness. The words defining the testimony echo its reality. The words are not to be confused with that reality itself. He continues: "We cling to it always, for as long as we endure, storing it up as protection against the terrors that will befall us. For truly, it renders faith resolute, opens up spiritual virtue, pleases *al-Raḥmān* (the All-Compassionate), repels *al-Shaytān* (the Satan)."

Going back to "befriending that by which the ignorant are estranged," one might say that this implies not just the trials of this life, which are a source of strength for the wise and repulsion for the ignorant, but also the inescapability of death. For the wise, this encounter with Allah for a blissful eternity means a release—in other words, a mode of *Tawḥīd*, whereby the soul enjoys ever-greater degrees of integration within higher principles. For the ignorant, by contrast, death is perceived as a frightful disintegration. It means the end of their world rather than the beginning of an endless life. This goes back to the earlier concept of death (while still going through) in life, described as *fanāʾ* (extinction, effacement, the "perishing" of egoistic consciousness in the face of the Absolute).
